==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Data centers are high consumers of power for cooling purposes, meaning there
is large savings potential with correct integration of free cooling and storage op-
tions. Several system layouts and conﬁgurations has been proposed, simulated
and evaluated based on both power consumption and life-time economy. The
layouts consider in which way the components of the cooling system, the dry
coolers, the sensible thermal storage and the vapor compression cycle should be
connected and furthermore, in which manner to control the systems depending
on ambient and desired cold water temperature.
The model is designed in EES, using a layer of if-statements to control the
system.
At relatively low and high temperatures, the system performances
similarly, while the large portion of typical temperatures oﬀers diﬀerent con-
trol schemes. Via macros and integration with Excel it was possible to perform
many full year simulations on hourly basis to determine yearly power con-
sumption. Based on the power consumption and component sizes, the system
economy can be evaluated taking into account the time value of money.
A number of temperature levels has been investigated for increasing dry cooler
and storage capacity. The results shows signiﬁcant power consumption and net
present values changes depending on the temperature. A cooling system based
only on the vapor compression cycle has a NPV of −447 M’DKK for the lowest
temperature level, while implementing a storage and dry cooler can reduce this
to −411 M’DKK. For higher temperature levels, it is possible to reduce this to
as low as −57 M’DKK, while still producing cooling water at 30◦C. The large
diﬀerence can be found in the amount of cooling the dry coolers are able to
provide.

Preface
A long journey is about to reach its ﬁnal destination. A journey of tears and
blood - properly spiced with joy, knowledge and new friendships. With this
work I formally end my time in the education system with a master’s degree
in ’Sustainable energy’ with a special focus on thermal energy technologies.
Trough my time at DTU competent help and guidance have always been given
plenty by the staﬀat the Section of Thermal Energy - for that I am grateful.
A special thank goes to my supervisors Brian Elmegaard and Torben Schmidt
Ommen for guidance through the last months. And thanks to Niels Houbak
from Ramboll for inputs and good conversations about all and nothing.
At the end, thanks to you, reader. If you are reading this line after the others,
you at least read one page of my thesis. Thank You.
Rasmus Øxenhave

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Data traﬃc
In the more and more digitalized world the need for internet and data traﬃc
capacity is larger than ever - resulting in an increasing need for data centers.
These facilities centralize IT operations and equipment to store, manage and
distribute data more eﬃciently.
On the global scale the data traﬃc in data centers has increased rapidly the
last years and the trend is expected to continue, see Figure 1.1. A forecast
by Cisco1 predicts that the global data center traﬃc increases from2 3 ZB/yr
to 8.5 ZB/yr from 2013-2018, which correspond to a compound annual growth
rate, CAGR3, equal to 23%. The major contribution is traﬃc from the cloud
based data centers, which almost four-fold in the period (CAGR = 32%). Only
a small increase in the traditional server capacity is expected (CAGR=8%). In
2013 cloud-computing accounted for 53% of total data traﬃc, this number
is expected to be 76% in 2018. Cisco predicts that the number of assigned
workloads, tasks, for the traditional option decreases be by CAGR=-2% [2],
the traﬃc however is expected to increase due to a larger internet penetration
globally.
Consequences of more data traﬃc
Clients expect fast and reliable data traﬃc, safe storage capacity and fast pro-
cessing. An endless struggle for the companies running the centers. In order
to deal with the problem larger and better data centers are build and exist-
ing are upgraded to follow the development. Changes can be within hardware
and/or IT architecture. The architecture of the cloud-based and traditional
is diﬀerent. Cloud computing, often referred to as simply “the cloud”, is the
delivery of on-demand computing resources over the Internet on a pay-for-use
1. “Cisco designs and sells broad lines of products, provides services and delivers integrated
solutions to develop and connect networks around the world, building the Internet” [1]

3. CAGR =

basis [3], this architecture allows rapid allocation of resources leading to higher
eﬃciency. The traditional option is more like pay-for-capacity.
Cloud service data centres are often of a size that justify the term mega data
centers. This refers directly to the physical dimensions, as these have in order
of tens of thousands servers and consume tens of MW peak power. It is bene-
ﬁcial to make cloud servers large as much of the cloud software use each other,
therefore the latency and operational cost can be minimized due to no need of
internet transmission lines [4].
According to the development of IT equipment, an upgrade often results in a
higher energy density - which leads to a higher power consumption. The power
used diﬀuses as heat proportionally. The heat that must be removed for the
hardware to operate properly. It is estimated that hardware upgrades and ex-
pansions can cause the heat production rate to doubled every two years [5]. An
experimental study shows a strong correlation between power consumption and
the data traﬃc [6], thus one can assume the power consumption will increase
in a similar trend. The global data center power consumption doubled from
2005-2010 [7]. Ebrahimi et al. [8] refers to an article stating that the global
peak power consumption in 2012 was 30 GW.
Eﬃcient cooling - a source of energy savings
The increased power consumption must be secured by power system operator,
but the cooling demand does also increase - a task that is dealt with at site.
It is of high importance to have a decent waste heat management. One of the
performance parameters is the PUE (power usage eﬀectiveness).
PUE = Total facility power
IT equipment power
(1.1)
The facility consumption covers cooling, lighting, emergency power supply and
the IT equipment. The most operation cost optimal solution is the minimal
target PUE ≃1 (no consumption for other than IT equipment). Data cen-
ter operators strive to minimize the energy consumption while maximizing the
performance of active IT equipment [5]. Table 1.1 shows examples of the en-
ergy distribution based on literature, the cooling being a large share of the
==PAGE:17==

total energy consumption, for which reason the potential of energy savings is
signiﬁcant.

2.2
Other studies indicates that the PUE world average is 1.9 [9] and typical PUE
values are 1.7-2.0 while for better managed data centers 1.4-1.6 [10]. Ineﬃcient
data centers have PUE around 2.0-3.0 [4].
The high energy consumption for cooling is mainly due to general use of tradi-
tional vapor compression refrigeration systems. They need to operate all year
round to serve the cooling demand, even though night and winter ambient
temperatures would allow for free cooling [7]. Free cooling is the utilization of
the cold ambient conditions by means of low power consumption equipment.
1.2
Objectives
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate the beneﬁts and feasibility of in-
troducing thermal storage capacity in combination with free cooling for a data
center cooling solution located in Denmark.
“Is it feasible to implement seasonal thermal storage capacity in
combination with free cooling for large scale low grade waste heat
data centers?”
“Which role does the sizes of storage, free coolers and vapor com-
pression cycle play?”
“How should the layout of such a system be?”
In order to answer the questions, quasi-static thermodynamic modeling will be
applied on diﬀerent plant layouts on hourly basis using Engineering Equation
Solver (EES). EES is a general equation-solving program that can numerically
solve thousands of coupled non-linear algebraic and diﬀerential equations. Fur-
thermore economical valuations will be conducted. The investigation can be
divided into two parts, which might not provide the same results.
1. Minimizing energy consumption
Minimizing energy consumption for data center cooling.
No price considerations, thermo-optimum.
2. Maximizing NPV (Net Present Value)
Maximizing the net present value for the cooling project
CAPEX, OPEX, lifetime is to be considered.
The ﬁndings are direct applicable for other industries and purposes within the
same temperature and load frame such as plastic, pharmaceutical, process or
food industries.
==PAGE:18==
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Case description
2.1
Data center layouts
Data centers can depending on the size and purpose have very diﬀerent layouts.
Common for all is that a number of servers and other IT hardware are placed in
the same location. The hardware has a form factor that ﬁts into standardized
rack mounting systems. The racks are used to provide easy access to hardware,
a compact design and simpliﬁed cabling as well as noise damping and better
cooling. Diﬀerent types of racks are on the market, from diﬀerent manufactures
and for diﬀerent cooling strategies. An example of rack arrangement can be
seen in Figure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Example of a data center inside1.
In traditional cooling solutions the rack rows are placed on a raised ﬂoor with
perforated tiles. A ’Computer Room Air Conditioner’ (CRAC) provides a cold
air ﬂow under the ﬂoor. A CRAC cools air by means of a refrigeration cycle.
The cold air ﬂow through the perforation to what is called a cold aisle. Fans
mounted at the racks draw the cold air across the IT equipment and exhaust
the hot air at the back to the hot aisle from which leads back the CRAC. The
air is recirculated inside the building, see Figure 2.2(a). This system works
well for low-medium sized data centers.
Increasing the air ﬂow rate in the
racks with extra fans is not a solution for high thermal loads [5].
Christy and Abimannan [5] proposes two alternative cooling systems utilizing
1. Used with permission from EdgeConneX Inc.
5
==PAGE:20==
6
CHAPTER 2. CASE DESCRIPTION
the ambient air. The ﬁrst solution, referred to as an air economizer, add ambi-
ent air to the server room if the ambient temperature is below the server room.
An ’Air Handle Unit’ (AHU) ﬁlters and prepare the air, see Figure 2.2(b). This
compared to a water economizer where an extra coil has been implemented in
the CRAC. The coil is connected to an external water loop with a cooling
tower, see Figure 2.2(c). The article ﬁnds that both air and water economizers
have a signiﬁcantly lower power consumption than a traditional CRAC, and
that water outperform air. A combination of cooling towers and chillers are
widely used for large scale data centers. [7].
Directing ambient air directly to the racks requires reliable control and sensing
devices. Humidity and cleanness need to be regulated as the air has direct
contact to the IT equipment to avoid corrosion and short circuits. The air
supply temperature to the cold aisles are in the range 15 −32 ◦C [11].
(a) Traditional [5]
(b) Air economizer [5]
(c) Water economizer [5]
(d) Water cooled servers [11]
Figure 2.2: Data center cooling solutions from literature.
Water can also be used as heat transfer media in data centers but is still novelty.
Iyengar et al. [11] demonstrate that it is possible to run a complete chillerless
cooling system (No CRAC). Cold water is produced with dry coolers, and a
buﬀer tank stores the water for later use. The water is pumped from the buﬀer
tank directly to the racks. Each rack has separate water-to-air heat exchangers
cooling the circulating air inside the racks.
The modeling and experiments
showed a that the power consumption for cooling was 3.4% of the IT consump-
tion - a signiﬁcant drop compared to the typical 40% from Table 1.1. Gao et al.
[12] did similar ﬁndings.
==PAGE:21==
2.1. DATA CENTER LAYOUTS
7
Another study by Iyengar et al. [13] compares four diﬀerent cooling systems
to the CRAC scenario. An evaporative airside economizer (EASE) rely com-
pletely on the ambient air. The air is conditioned to the right temperature
and humidity by an evaporative cooling system and heating element before it
enters the rack in a conventional aisle setup, see Figure 2.3(a). The method is
used in the indirect airside economizer, Figure 2.3(b). The diﬀerence is that
the air inside the server room does not mix with the ambient air. The last two
are with water cooled racks and buﬀer tank. One of them uses an evapora-
tive dry cooler (EDRHx), Figure 2.3(c), where water is sprayed onto the heat
exchanger surface increasing the heat transfer rate. The other is a simple dry
cooler similar to the setup from Iyengars experiments (DRHx), Figure 2.2(d).
The power consumption is presented in Table 2.1. The most power eﬃcient is
the water cooled solutions.

Zhang et al. [7] did a review on system conﬁgurations using free cooling. Free
==PAGE:22==
8
CHAPTER 2. CASE DESCRIPTION
cooling is commonly known as an economizer cycle that uses the surrounding
environment as a cooling source (air, water, rock).
Free cooling can either
work alone or in a hybrid combination with traditional refrigeration cycles.
The power consumption associated with free cooling is typically very small
compared to a traditional cooling system. Two main reasons why a traditional
cooling system consumes large quantites of energy:
1. High energy consumption from compressors that have to work all year
round, even when the ambient temperature is low (night and winter).
2. The hot air exhausted from racks can mix with the air in cold aisles and
cause higher air temperature into the rack.
Zhang et al. [7] ﬁnds that the options for free cooling can be divided into
three categories based on the cooling infrastructure in the data center. These
is summarized in Table 2.2. It could be air or water transferred to the racks
or heat pipes which is slowly getting into the market. Heat pipes are able to
transfer heat without external energy input. Fin arrays reject the heat to the
ambient by natural convection or forced if either the wind is blowing or fans
are added. Airside can both be direct if the ambient air is mix with the server
room air, or indirect if an air-to-air heat exchanger is implemented. The source
of cooling at waterside options can either come natural sources like seawater
and groundwater. Water-to-water heat exchangers are needed to separate the
natural source from the internal water loop in the server room. One has to
be aware of possible environmental damage or environment damaging the data
center if placed near sea. Alternatively the water can be cooled by means of
either air cooled with using dry coolers or a cooling tower.
Table 2.2: Diﬀerent types of data center free cooling systems [7]
FC Category
Type
Feature
Airside
Direct
Drawing the cold outside air directly inside
Indirect
Utilizing the outside air through heat exchanger
Waterside
Direct water cooled
Using natural cold water directly
Air cooled
Using dry cooler to cool the circulating water
Cooling tower
Using cooling tower to cool the circulating water
Heat pipe
Independent
With no mechanical refrigeration function
Integrated
Integrated with mechanical refrigeration system
Cold storage
Combined with cold storage system
Hybrid cooling is widely used as it provides free cooling with a back-up and
supplement cooling solution in a refrigeration cycle. Such a system switches
between the technologies according to the ambient climate conditions. However
the period for utilization of free cooling can be limited if the cut-in temperature
for the free cooling is set to a low temperature (e.g. 8 ◦C) [9]. Hybrid cooling
systems can produce the cooling cheaper on average than a direct expansion
refrigeration cycle [7].
The location of a data center plays a role for the power savings. The power
consumption of a traditional cooling system versus an air economizer setup like
Figure 2.2(b) has been conducted by Depoorter et al. [14]. Five diﬀerent loca-
tions in Europa was investigated: Barcelona, Amsterdam, London, Frankfurt
==PAGE:23==
2.1. DATA CENTER LAYOUTS
9
and Stockholm. Data centers located in north uses less power due to general
lower ambient temperatures. Similar ﬁnding was reported by Ge and Zheng
[15] for China. Typical chiller free operation is 5000-7000 hours in southern
and central China and more than 8500 in north. Kim et al. [9] also states that
the using the ambient air to free cooling is limited to cold regions.
Oró et al. [16] did a comprehensive review on data center energy eﬃciency
and integration with renewable energy sources. The energy consumption can
be unnecessary high due to poor air ﬂow management, packed racks and long
distances from racks to cooling source. Most data center cooling systems are
designed for the worst case scenario, where servers runs at 100% load and the
ambient temperature are high. This requires large components, which enhance
the investment and operational costs. Worst case scenarios rarely happens as
servers usually operate at a lower threshold (e.g. 80% [9]). Using water cooling
can eliminate the need for the CRAC, as the cooling water temperature can be
higher compared to the air temperature in an air cooled rack. If one has con-
cerns on leakage near the electronics a dielectric ﬂuid can be chosen instead of
water. A large focus is on reducing power consumption from cooling, however
the IT equipment and how it is operated impacts the cooling demand greatly.
Data workload optimization can help on this together with choosing hardware
with the highest possible eﬃciency.
Ebrahimi et al. [8] summarizes a lot of system conﬁgurations, typical temper-
atures and other key parameters as well as a list of process integration in a
large review. Except for air and water cooling, a brief discussion on two-phase
cooled systems are given. The data traﬃc increases and the hardware is up-
graded to higher energy dense models. From this follows a higher and higher
cooling demand. A media changing phase in the racks can give large saving
in power consumption as the mass ﬂow rate of coolant can be decreased a lot
due to the latent heat. Depending on the waste heat temperature diﬀerent
opportunities arise. It could be hot water production, low temperature district
heating, power plant feed water preheat, heat source for absorption refrigera-
tion or organic Rankine cycles for power production etc.
This literature review shows, that numerous proposals for cooling systems are
available. Some more energy eﬃcient than others, but only the imagination
sets the limits. It provides signiﬁcant energy savings to use free cooling no mat-
ter the solution. The waterside economizers is recommended by the American
Society of Heating, Refrigerating, and Air-Conditioning Engineers (ASHRAE),
and a survey showed that the most widely used economizer uses water [7].

2.2
Case boundaries
System design:
In these studies the internal layout of the data center is
omitted.
This means that the distribution of cooling to the racks are not
considered. It is assumed that the internal system needs cooling water at a
given temperature for a coil in a CRAC or a secondary loop circulating some
media directly to the computer equipment. The cooling system should contain
dry coolers2 (Water side air cooling), a vapor compression cycle (VCC) and
a thermal energy storage (ST). According to Zhang et al. [7], Table 2.2, it
deﬁnes the system as a waterside air cooled system. Other places it could be
mentioned as a hybrid cooling system or water economizing system [5]. A such
system does not introduce indoor disturbances like moist, dust and similar.
The case boundary is graphically shown in Figure 2.4. The data center acts like
a black box for which the cooling system must provide the to all time needed
cooling, ˙Qd, at the desired cold water supply temperature3, T3. The black box
returns hot water, T1, to the cooling system. The layout of the cooling system
is to be investigated in following chapters.

Figure 2.4: Case boundary
Load:
The main thermal load of the data center is from IT equipment, where
the thermal load is approximately equal to the power consumption of the IT
equipment. The power input to the electronics dissipate to heat which should
be removed in order to prevent heat building up IT equipment and eventually
to exceed the rated thermal design temperature. If the cooling system should
provide cooling for the rest of the building as well additional thermal loads
from lights, humans and heat transfer through the building envelope would be
added. In this project it is assumed only to be IT cooling. The equipment has
a nominal power consumption and an associated nominal heat generation.
Pnom,IT ≈˙Qnom,IT
(2.1)
It is not likely that the data center runs at full utilization as it decreases the
life time on IT equipment. Instead the operation runs within some utilization
thresholds given by the server architecture. It could be that the maximum
2. Dry coolers and free coolers are from now the same and used indiscriminately.
3. From here on also called the cooling system target temperature
==PAGE:25==
2.2. CASE BOUNDARIES
11
allowance for the data center is a threshold of 0.8, meaning that the maximum
load is 80% of the nominal load [9]. Most data centers and their cooling system
are designed for the worst case scenario potentially leading to oversized me-
chanical components enhancing both the investment and the operational cost
[16]. The worst case rarely happens [9].
For the studies in this report the load is set to a constant value - a value of
80% utilization.
˙Qd = 0.8 · ˙Qnom,IT = 10 MW
(2.2)
Data centers in the range 10-20 MW IT power consumption can have 100,000s
of servers [4].
Temperature levels:
The temperature limits for operating is 85 ◦C for pro-
cessing units and 45 ◦C for disk drives. Typical cold water supply temperatures
are in the interval 20 −60 ◦C for water cooled systems, while supplying for a
CRAC around 10 ◦C is typical [8]. The cooled water supply temperature, T3,
is chosen to be three diﬀerent levels 10, 20 and 30 ◦C. These temperatures are
assumed to satisfy the set point for IT operation temperature including losses
through heat exchangers for a given internal set up. The temperature increase
through the data center is assumed to be either 5 K and 15 K. The higher
the cold water supply temperature is the higher utilization of free cooling is
expected, see section 5.1.1.
The temperature levels for the investigations (T1/T3 ◦C):
15/10 ◦C
25/20 ◦C
35/30 ◦C

45/30 ◦C
The temperature levels covers a wide range and can be relevant for many other
applications in the industry or HVAC systems in buildings for the temperature
10 and 20 ◦C, as these are below the comfort temperature.
Location:
The data center located in the Copenhagen area or a location with
similar weather conditions. The Design Reference Year (DRY) for Copenhagen
is used for the simulations. The data is hourly (8760 h) and the data points
are averaged for ten measurements measured in the years 2003-2013 for that
particular hour. The temperature curve can be seen in Figure 2.5, where the
ﬁrst hour is 1. January 0.30 AM. In the late autumn, winter and start spring
months (November to mid April) the ambient temperature is almost all the
time below the coldest water target temperature (10 ◦C) indicating that the
potential for free cooling for sure can be used as the only source of cooling
in these months if suﬃcient dry coolers are installed. The higher the target
temperature, the higher the potential for complete free cooling gets.
In a
number of hours with ambient temperatures below the target temperature, the
capacity of the FC might be too large for the cooling demand. In these cases
one can either throttle the fans, thus decreasing the capacity and still keep the
target temperature, or try to store the cold water for later use. The question
is whether it is possible and aﬀordable to make large and eventually seasonal
storage.

2.3
Cooling system layout
In order to fulﬁll the purpose one has to consider how such a system should
be designed. The component arrangement, storage and heat transfer media,
design of the VCC, FC and storage, piping and last but not least a control
scheme are to be considered.
2.3.1
Media
Media inside the black box data center is not considered. As the waterside
economizers shows great potential for eﬃcient cooling, is water chosen as heat
transport media.
Water has high availability and relative low costs.
From
an environmental and safety point of view is water a good choice due to the
non-toxicity and inﬂammability. Furthermore, water has a high heat capacity.
2.3.2
FC and VCC arrangement
If one have two components who are to be directly connected to each other,
the maximum number of combinations is three. Either one can choose a series
connection with the FC before VCC, Figure 2.6(a), or the other way around:
VCC before FC, Figure 2.6(b). In both cases the cooling water mass ﬂow rate is
the same in both components. The last alternative is to place them parallel as
shown in Figure 2.6(c). In this case the mass ﬂow rate from the heat source are
divided into two streams for the FC and VCC, respectively. The split ratio can
be chosen freely, however it is important that the mixing temperature of the
two streams equals the desired target temperature for the cold water supply. It
might not be trivial to say which of the conﬁgurations that perform best, which

here means provide the lowest power consumption, in a full year operation
scenario. It is however possible to do some considerations. It is desirable to have
a high COP of the VCC. The Carnot COP, the theoretical highest attainable
COP, can provide a frame of reference for comparison, equation (2.3).
TL
and TH should be representative temperatures in K for the process and for a
refrigeration cycle it could be the condensing and evaporation temperature.

(2.3)
The condensation temperature of the VCC is determined by the ambient tem-
perature and the compressor displacement ratio, and the evaporation tem-
perature of the cooling water outlet temperature. Assuming the condensing
temperature to be the same for the three cases one should strive for a high
evaporation temperature.
With this perspective the three conﬁgurations behave a bit diﬀerent. TE =
T3 −∆TE for Conﬁg A, where as ∆TE is the pinch temperature diﬀerence in
evaporator. In Conﬁg B, where the VCC is placed as the ﬁrst component, TE
will be dependent on the temperature, T2, between the two components in the
same manor with a ∆TE. As T2 > T3 the COP will be better compared to
Conﬁg A. The more heat the FC transfers the higher T2, thus an increasing
COP - where it will remain the same in the other two conﬁgurations. For the
parallel setup, Conﬁg C, it is also T3 which sets the limit, which gives the same
COP as conﬁg A.
Regarding the FC one strives to have as many operation hours as possible,
but depending on the conﬁguration the ambient temperature can constitute a
constraint. If the FC is to cool the water to T3, then the FC is constraint for
Tamb ≥T3 (Conﬁg B and C). If the system is such that it is satisfying that
the FC just provide some cooling even though the water outlet temperature
does not meet the target temperature T3, then the system works for Tamb ≤T1
(Conﬁg A and C).
In Conﬁg C two possible scenarios appear. Either both the FC and VCC de-
liver water at temperature equal to T3, in that case T3 is the constraining factor
for the FC. Otherwise the one let the VCC deliver water below T3 for those
hours where the FC only can provide water temperature above T3 - in this case
the constrain is T1. The mass ﬂow split rate must adapt such that the mixing
temperature equals the target temperature T3. More of this in section 9.3.

Preliminary one should go for a system that allow utilization of FC to the high-
est possible ambient temperature, as FC is a relative cheap cooling method.
Still a good COPV CC is also likely preferable.
None of the conﬁgurations are an obvious choice, why all three must be investi-
gated. That said, Conﬁg A and C can use FC to some extent at a higher Tamb,
hence those are the most interesting choices. The overall goal is to produce
cheap cooling, why use of FC should be maximized and VCC minimized. The
best guess at this point is that inﬂuence of the poorer COPV CC is neglectable
and becomes even more neglectable, when a thermal energy storage is added
to the system. The storage purpose is to postpone the use of VCC or even
eliminate the need.
2.3.3
Storage coupling
The implementation of storage to the cooling system follows the same method-
ology as the arrangement of the free cooler and vapor compression cycle. The
storage are to be coupled with a cooling producing arrangement (CPA), which
can be any of the previously discussed ones. Two units give three possibilities.
Two series and one parallel.
The ﬁrst is a series setup with the storage placed inline after the CPA as shown
in Figure 2.7(a). The storage practically acts like a large pipe. The mass ﬂow
rate in and out of the storage are the same, and so are the temperatures,
T2 = T3. There is no possibility to store for later use - a bad solution. A
variant of this setup with storage abilities is presented in Figure 2.7(b). A hot
by-pass of the system are added. The cooling systems focus should be to pro-
duce the coldest water within the allowed interval (0◦C < T2 < T3) lowering
the storage mean temperature. A mixing value ensures the correct temperature
to the data center by mixing water from the storage with hot water from the
data center, T1. As long as the temperature in the storage is below T3 it is
possible to store for later use.

The next coupling is the opposite series connection with a inline storage be-

more cooling than needed for the data center (higher mass ﬂow rate from T1 to
T3) it is possible to start circulating the cold water back using the cold return
line. The cold water is mixing with the hot water T1. The water entering
the storage depends to the mass ﬂow ratio and the temperatures T1 and T3.
The mean temperature in the storage will decrease. The temperature of the
storage will gradually increase until it reaches T1 with cooling system are not
producing excess cooling. A lower temperature into CPA, T2, could lower the
power consumption. The storage can never act as only source of cooling as the
temperature in the storage always is above T3.

Chapter 3
Thermodynamic method
This section discuss the thermodynamic modeling and the associated formulas
and deﬁnitions. The systems consist of three main components: a dry cooler,
a vapour compression cycle and a cold water storage tank.
3.1
Vapor compression cycle
3.1.1
General
The four system designs, that is to be investigated, requires a water chiller
based on a vapor compression cycle. A thermal machine which transfers heat
from a low temperature heat source to a high temperature heat sink, reducing
or maintaining the source temperature.
A general schematic of a thermal
machine is shown in Figure 3.1. Between the two reservoirs is the refrigeration
cycle placed.
High temperature heat sink
˙Qsink
Low temperature heat source
˙Qsource
˙Et
Figure 3.1: Thermal machine
Due to the second law of thermodynamics the heat transfer is only possible if
additional work, ˙Et, is added to the system. The ﬁrst law of thermodynamics
dictates the conservation of energy in the system: the sum of energy ﬂows into
the system should equal to the energy ﬂows out of the system. The useful
cooling rate is ˙Qsource and ˙Qsink the heat rejected to the heat sink.
˙Qsource + ˙Et = −˙Qsink
(3.1)
One of the many refrigeration cycles that could be chosen is the counterclock-
wise operated Carnot cycle, a cycle that represents the thermophysical limit. It
17
==PAGE:32==
18
CHAPTER 3. THERMODYNAMIC METHOD
is only possible from a theoretical point of view as it assumes full reversibility,
which obviously does not apply in practice. The cycle operates per deﬁnition
between two sink and source temperatures, Tsink and Tsource. The working
ﬂuid undergoes four reversible processes, Figure 3.2(a):
1. Isentropic compression increasing the temperature from Tsource to Tsink.
2. Isothermal compression at Tsink rejecting heat to sink, ˙Qsink.
3. Isentropic expansion from Tsink to Tsource. Without heat transfer.
4. Isothermal expansion at Tsource absorbing heat from source, ˙Qsource.
A cycle performance parameter is the ratio between useful cooling capacity and
the total energy input. This parameter is called the Coeﬃcient Of Performance,
COP. It can be shown that the COP of the Carnot cycle is only based on the
temperature levels (in Kelvin),
COPC =
˙Qsource
˙Et
=
Tsource
Tsink −Tsource
(3.2)
In order to convert the cycle to a more realistic cycle, a ’total Carnot eﬃciency’
can be applied. This eﬃciency take the losses associated with a real cycle into
account as well as the cycle shift from Carnot to real.
By this method an
estimate of the cycle COP could be operating between sink and source.
COPt = COPC · ηCt
(3.3)
The total Carnot eﬃciency can be expressed as the product of the eﬃciency of
energy conversion from electric power to work added to the refrigeration cycle.
ηCt = ηelm · ηmt · ηs,c · ηCd
(3.4)
Where ηelm is the eﬃciency of the electric motor driving the compressor. ηmt
is the mechanical power transmission from motor to compressor, which can
be assumed close to 1, if the they share the same shaft. In other cases where
the belt or chain transmission is used one has to consult the literature for
correlation for eﬃciency.
ηs,c is the isentropic eﬃciency of the compressor,
stating how much extra work must be added to the ﬂuid due to the losses in
the real compressor.
The last term in equation (3.4) is call ’Carnot eﬃciency of refrigerant’ and is an
expression for how similar the real cycle is to the Carnot cycle with respect to
COP. It is deﬁned as the ratio between the COP of the refrigerant in the real
cycle (using enthalpy diﬀerences and cycle temperatures) and the COPC (using
sink and source temperatures). COPd depends on the temperature levels and
the shape of the property plot.
ηCd = COPd
COPC
(3.5)
Typical values for the eﬃciencies are presented in Table 3.1. The total Carnot
eﬃciency is typically in the range 0.4-0.6 [17] and depends on the type of cycle
and characteristics such as subcooling, superheating, media and components.
==PAGE:33==
3.1. VAPOR COMPRESSION CYCLE
19
ηCt increases with plant size as large components tends to have higher eﬃcien-
cies.
Table 3.1: Typical eﬃciencies [17]
ηCt
0.4 - 0.6
ηCd
0.6 - 0.9
ηs,c
0.6 - 0.8
ηmt
0.5 - 1.0
ηelm
0.9 - 1.0
The VCC needed as chiller does not set high requirements. It should be able to
lift the temperature from a temperature just below the cold water target tem-
perature (source) to a temperature a little above ambient temperature (sink).
A choice of real cycle for this purpose could be a single stage VCC - a reversed
Rankine cycle - as the temperature lift is relatively small. The Carnot cycle
shows in general great similarity with a basic VCC.
A basic VCC is a single stage refrigeration cycle where the subcooling ∆TSC =
0 K and a vapor super heat ∆TSH = 0 K. The compressor is ideal. And no
pressure losses occur. The process steps are somewhat identical.
1. Adiabatic and isentropic compression from Psat(TE) to Psat(TC).
2. Isobaric condensation at Psat(TC) rejecting heat to sink, ˙Qsink.
3. Isenthalpic expansion from TC to TE. Without heat transfer.
4. Isobaric expansion at TE absorbing heat from source, ˙Qsource.
Most of the heat transfer in the single stage VCC does also happen at constant
temperature - more speciﬁc the condensation and evaporation temperature, TC
and TE respectively. The similarity between the Carnot cycle and a basic VCC
can be seen in Figure 3.2. The diﬀerences is in step
1 and
3 . The Carnot
cycle compresses the ﬂuid to it reach a the temperature Tsink, where the VCC
compress to saturation pressure, this causes the compressor outlet temperature
to be higher than TC. The isenthalpic expansion in step 3 introduce entropy
generation lowering the useful cooling output ˙Qsource. The expansion makes
the cycle irreversible.
The area beneath
4 in the (T, s)-diagram for the Carnot cycle can be inter-
preted as the useful cooling rate, ˙Q2, while the area ( 2 - 4 ) is the total energy
input needed, ˙Et. From this it is clear that the isenthalpic expansion and extra
compression results in a lower COP for the VCC than Carnot, thus ηCd < 1.
The similarity of the Carnot and basic VCC yields a high ηCd. Normally the
VCC should operate such that the evaporation is below the source temperature
and the condensing temperature above ambient for the heat transfer to obey
the 2nd law of thermodynamics.

3.1.2
Equations in model
In the modeling it is assumed that the VCC is a single stage cycle and that it
can be modeled according to the described method using the alternative COPC
deﬁnition.
Schematics of the heat transfer in the refrigeration cycle is shown in ( ˙Q, T)-
diagrams in Figure 3.3. Water and refrigerant exchange heat in the evaporator.
==PAGE:35==
3.1. VAPOR COMPRESSION CYCLE
21
The evaporation temperature is always set by the desired water outlet temper-
ature, Tw,out, and a pinch temperature diﬀerence, ∆Tp,E. The pinch temper-
ature diﬀerence is a design parameter. A low pinch temperature diﬀerences is
desirable as the losses during heat transfer are reduced, but the price of this is
a larger heat exchanger (larger UA), thus higher capital investment.
The condenser reject heat to the ambient air. The condensing temperature, TC
is ∆TEtoC higher than TE. ∆TEtoC is deﬁned as the maximum value of either
Tamb + ∆TC or a minimum allowed temperature diﬀerence, which in practice
is determined by the speciﬁc compressor in the cycle. There is a lower limit of
the pressure diﬀerence, thus the temperature diﬀerence.
∆TEtoC = max
Tamb + ∆TC
∆TEtoCmin

(3.9)
Investigations show that the optimal condensing temperature with respect to
economy is TC = Tamb + ∆TC, where ∆TC = 10 K [18].

The inﬂuence of the of the max function is that the performance decreases
for increasing ambient temperatures. The evaporation temperature is always
constant given by Tw,out. Figure 3.4 shows how the TC changes for TE = 5 ◦C
and TE = 20 ◦C. Up to a point TC is constant due to the ∆TEtoCmin but at
15 ◦C the ambient temperature starts to be dominant for the low TE. This
point is 30 ◦C for the other case. The ambient temperature starts to force TC
up, which due to the COP2C deﬁnition decreases the COP of the system. If
one operate at a high TE the ambient inﬂuence is not signiﬁcant as only few
hours of the year have really high ambient temperature. The graphs reveals
that a high TE is preferable.

Using the same approach on the temperatures as Granryd calculations of ηCd

in the low range, but still acceptable, but should not be lower.

3.1.3
Sensitivity analysis
The input parameters for the vapor compression cycle are assumed values. An
sensitivity analysis can show the inﬂuence of the parameters to the COP of
the cycle. In inputs from Table 3.2 yields a COP = 7.2. The four parameters
∆TC, ∆Tp,E, ∆TEtoCmin and ηCt are changed one at the time. The results are
presented in Figure 3.6.
It is clear from the ﬁgure that both the total Carnot eﬃciency and the mini-
mum temperature rise from evaporation to condensation temperature has the
largest impact on the COP. The eﬃciency has a direct proportional as seen
in equation (3.12). The eﬃciency can be increased by acquiring better compo-
nents. The COP would increase signiﬁcant if the parameter ∆TEtoCmin was
decreased, however due to the pressure ratio of the compressor it is not likely
to be possible. Increasing the temperature diﬀerence would have a signiﬁcant
eﬀect.
The pinch temperature diﬀerence in the evaporator, ∆Tp,E, and temperature
diﬀerence between condensing and ambient temperature, ∆TC, are not impor-
tant with this model formulation. The changes in COP are ±1.7% and ±0%,
respectively.
The conclusion is that one should pay attention to the pressure ratio and eﬃ-
ciency.

3.2

3.2.1
Energy equations
Under the assumption of no heat dissipation to the surroundings the heat drawn
from the hot ﬂuid, ˙Qh, is equal to the heat absorbed by the cold ﬂuid, ˙Qc, due
to the energy balance, equation (3.14).
0 = ˙Qh + ˙Qc +
*0
˙Qdis
(3.14)
As ˙Qh =
˙Qc one can denote a common heat transfer rate,
˙Q. The energy
equations for the hot and cold side are shown in equation (3.15) and (3.16),
respectively. The heat transfer rate is given by the mass ﬂow rate times the
enthalpy diﬀerence of the ﬂuid. The enthalpy is a function of two ﬂuid proper-
ties, which for a non-phase changing region could be temperature and pressure.
Subscript h and c is hot and cold side respectively, i inlet and o outlet.
˙Q = ˙mh · (hh,i −hh,o)
(3.15)
˙Q = ˙mc · (hc,o −hc,i)
(3.16)
These equations does not take the heat exchanger design and the corresponding
operational limitations into account. They can be just ﬁne if one is to calculate
a design point, but it is not suﬃcient if part load is to be investigated. For the
part load case equations that connect the heat transfer rate with the geometry
are necessary. The additional equations could be the following: The convective
heat transfer for the hot and cold side, equation (3.17)-(3.18), or conduction
in the wall from hot to cold side, equation (3.19).
˙Q = ¯hc · Ac · ( ¯Tc −¯Tw,c)
(3.17)
˙Q = ¯hh · Ah · ( ¯Tw,h −¯Th)
(3.18)
˙Q =
¯k
δw
· ¯A · ( ¯Tw,h −¯Tw,c)
(3.19)
The convective heat transfer coeﬃcients, ¯h, can be found from empirical corre-
lations based on geometry and the ﬂuid dynamics. Typically these correlations
provide an average Nusselt number deﬁned as the ratio of convective and con-
ductive heat transfer of the ﬂuid, equation (3.20), from which the convective
heat transfer coeﬃcient can be solved. The ﬂuid conductance, k, is a table value
and L is the length of the heat transfer area in the ﬂow direction. Correlations
can be found in heat transfer books (e.g. [20]). The equations (3.17)-(3.19)
relies on average temperatures, thus provide average coeﬃcients. If a more ac-
curate calculation is needed the heat exchanger can be discritized into smaller
sections. More sections decrease the error. All three are needed unless a wall
temperature is given or assumed.
NuL =
¯hL
k
(3.20)
Alternatively the LMTD-method can be used for steady state purposes, which
essentially express the same as the equations (3.17)-(3.19). The heat transfer
==PAGE:40==
26
CHAPTER 3. THERMODYNAMIC METHOD
in the heat exchanger can be calculated as the heat transfer capacitance, UA,
times an appropriate mean temperature between terminal temperatures of the
hot and cold ﬂuid, equation (3.21) [20]. UA is the product of a heat transfer
area, A, and an overall heat transfer coeﬃcient, U, from hot to cold ﬂuid.
˙Q = UA · ∆Tm
(3.21)
The UA is an expression for the heat exchanger size and layout.
Using the LMTD-method assumes following for the heat transfer [20]:
1. The heat exchanger is perfectly insulated. Heat does only transfer be-
tween the ﬂuids.
2. Axial conduction along the heat exchanger material is negligible.
3. Potential and kinetic energy changes are negligible.
4. The speciﬁc heats of the ﬂuids are constant.
5. The overall heat transfer coeﬃcient is constant.
3.2.2
Logarithmic mean temperature diﬀerence
The LMTD, equation (3.22), has generally been accepted as driving force (mean
temperature diﬀerence) in equation (3.21), a driving force depeding on the four
terminal temperatures.
∆Tlm = ∆T1 −∆T2
ln
∆T1
∆T2

(3.22)
The temperature diﬀerences ∆T1 and ∆T2 is the terminal temperature diﬀer-
ence at each side of the heat exchanger. Both must be positive at all times to
avoid violation of the 2nd law of thermodynamics. In the rest of the project
counter ﬂow is assumed.
∆T1 ≡
(
Th,i −Tc,o,
if counter ﬂow.
Th,i −Tc,i,
if parallel ﬂow.
(3.23)
∆T2 ≡
(
Th,o −Tc,i,
if counter ﬂow.
Th,o −Tc,o,
if parallel ﬂow.
(3.24)
Since the 1970 the literature has proposed alternatives for the LMTD formula-
tion, as it can cause problems for equation-oriented calculation programs [21].
The problem lies within the logarithm in the denominator.
For example cases where ∆T1 = ∆T2 which happens if the capacity rate on
both sides are equal. Here does the LMTD formulation fail as the denominator
ratio ∆T1/∆T2 = 1 and ln (1) = 0. The same problem can be observed with
the denominator in the ratio if either ratio ∆T1 or ∆T2 approaches zero - in
such cases the numerical solver in the software can have problems with conver-
gence.
==PAGE:41==
3.2. DRY COOLER

Awad [22] have collected a number of correlations for the LMTD from the
literature, where the logarithm as well as temperature diﬀerences in the de-
nominator are eliminated. The correlations are presented in Table 3.5, where
the temperature diﬀerences ∆T1 and ∆T2 follows the deﬁnitions in equation
(3.23) and (3.24). First Underwood presented a simple formula in 1970 for
the approximation with direct use of ∆T1 and ∆T2. Years later in 1984 Pa-
terson gave his suggestion, a solution based on the arithmetic and geometric
mean temperature diﬀerences (AMTD and GMTD), Chen came with a vari-
ant three years later. The same year Chen also presented an improvement to
Underwoods suggestion.

The correlations are plotted to visualize how well they ﬁt the LMTD in real
values, Figure 3.7, and the deviation in percentage, Figure 3.8. The plots shows
that use of AMTD would provide a signiﬁcant error, however one can still use
it to calculate the guess values for the numerical calculations as it is a rather
simple expression. Compared to the AMTD the GMTD seems to be a better
choice as it follows the right trend in absolute value, Figure 3.7. The error is
slightly smaller, but still signiﬁcant.
The correlations from Underwood, Paterson and Chen do ﬁt the LMTD quite
well. In general the worst approximation is when one of the temperature diﬀer-
ences approaches zero and the other one is large1. It happens for ∆T1/∆T2 →0
or ∆T1/∆T2 →∞. This observation matches the ﬁndings of Pettersson [23].
The AMTD, Underwood and Paterson correlations overestimates the LMTD,
while Chen’s 1st and the GMTD underestimates. Chen’s 2nd overestimate for
very low ratios and have a slightly underestimate for ratios the range 0.2 −9,
but the deviation makes it almost negligible. An underestimation of LMTD will
1. (∆T1/∆T2 →0) if (∆T1 →0) and/or (∆T2 →∞)
(∆T1/∆T2 →∞) if (∆T1 →∞) and/or (∆T2 →0)

lead to a overestimation in the required heat transfer area - which in practice
can be argued as preferable as it gives the heat exchanger some overcapacity.
Chen [24] did similar investigations for four diﬀerent cases of ∆T1 and ∆T2
with the conclusion that the his 2nd approximation provided the best results.
Based on the ﬁndings and the literature Chen’s 2nd approximation for the
LMTD will be used as the mean temperature diﬀerence in equation (3.21).

3.2.3
Heat transfer capacitance
The unit of the heat transfer capacitance, UA, is kW/K and is an expression
for the heat exchanger size and layout. The calculation of the UA for an air
to water ﬁnned pipe heat exchanger, equation (3.25), consist of ﬁve thermal
resistances from the hot water to the colder air. Forced convection will be on
both sides due to either fans or pumps. Surface fouling - a resistance that
increases over time as dirt settle, and ﬁnally wall conduction. Typically the
fouling and conduction are neglected - fouling because it is hard to predict
the value and conduction due to the small thermal resistance compared with
the convection terms [20]. All properties are typically evaluated at the mean
temperature of the ﬂuid. Cold side mean and hot side mean.
1

(3.25)
On the the airside the ﬁns provide a signiﬁcantly larger heat transfer area
compared to the pipe wall. An overall surface eﬃciency, η0, is introduced to
reduce the eﬀective surface area of an array of ﬁns, thus compensating for
the fact that the temperature distribution in the ﬁns are not constant and
equal to the base temperature. The ﬁns have a higher temperature at the base
(connection to outer pipe wall) than at the tip. η0 is deﬁned as the total heat
actually transferred divided by maximum possible heat transfer where the total
ﬁn area has Tfin = Tbase.
η0 ≡
˙Qt
˙Qmax
= 1 −N Afin
At
(1 −ηf)
(3.26)
N is the number of ﬁns, Afin the single ﬁn area, and the total area At =
(N Afin +Abase), where a Abase is the visible area of the pipe between the ﬁns.
ηf is the eﬃciency of a single ﬁn. ηf is the ratio of actual heat transfer from
the ﬁn and the heat transfer is all the ﬁn were base temperature. ∆Tm,fin =
¯Tfin −Tamb and ∆Tbase = Tbase −Tamb
ηf ≡
˙Qfin
˙Qideal fin
= ∆Tm,fin
∆Tbase
(3.27)
The convective heat transfer coeﬃcients, h, for both the air and water side can
be found from empirical correlation for the speciﬁc geometry and ﬂow condi-
tions. Typically such an air/water heat exchanger is a cross ﬂow variant. The
pipes can be arranged as a single pipe or in banks both aligned or staggered,
where the ﬁns can allow or avoid the air to mix. The ﬁn geometry has multiple
options: Straight, wavy, waﬄed and more exotic variants. One has to ﬁnd a
correlation that matches just that geometry that is to be investigated. The
same goes for the waterside. The pipes can be smooth, be rough or have a
special surface for increasing the heat transfer coeﬃcient.
It is deﬁnitely not trivial to estimate the UA. To avoid concerns of these ge-
ometry and ﬂow questions one can turn to heat exchanger manufacturers, who
have conducted detail calculations, CFD and/or experiments on the products.
==PAGE:44==

CHAPTER 3. THERMODYNAMIC METHOD
From data sheets or product software it is possible to get information about
the UA or design data, that makes it possible to calculate it. An example of
a drycooler manufacturer is Güntner. In Appendix B UA-calculations can be
seen for several models from the Güntner double coil dry coolers catalog. This
is a type of a V-shaped heat exchanger put together in sections. Each V-section
has two fans. The results show that the UA increases linear with the number
of sections, Figure 3.9. The ﬂow conditions in each section is almost equiv-
alent which gives a constant U, so the heat transfer area, A, is the changing
parameter. This is for the design case.

3.2. DRY COOLER

dry cooler which forms reference for modeling [25]. 16 fans are place on a 2×8
model providing approximately 160kW/K.
Nfan =
16 fans
160 kW/K · UA100
(3.32)
The total nominal air mass ﬂow rate, ˙m100 and fan power, ˙W100 is found by
multiplying the values from Table 3.6 with Nfan.
At some point it might be necessary to adjust the capacity of the heat ex-
changers to match the cooling demand. The colder the ambient temperature
becomes the higher the heat transfer capacity is. At some point the free cool-
ers are able to exceed the cooling demand. Two options arise: 1) Store excess
cooling for later use 2) Throttle the heat exchanger capacity. The ﬁrst solution
is mostly desired, however throttling is also needed for the hours where the
storage is full or nearly full. The UA is decreased when throttling.
Throttling of is done by changing the ﬂow condition on one or either side - the
heat transfer area can not easily be adjusted. As a rule of thumb the convective
heat transfer coeﬃcient hair ≪hwater, so even though the airside area is large,
then the thermal resistance of the airside convection will be the dominant. This
means that changes in water ﬂow conditions does provide a change in UA, but
not as signiﬁcant as a similar percent vise change in air ﬂow.
Three options are available for fan control: Fixed speed, variable speed and step
vise speed change. Fixed speed is a simple ON/OFF control of the fans, a such
is somewhat equivalent to decreasing the heat transfer area. Variable speed fan
control adjust the rotational speed of the fans from 0-100% - the heat transfer
coeﬃcient changes as the air ﬂow condition changes. The last option adjust
the fan speed in maybe two or three predeﬁned steps (e.g. 0/33/66/100%)
and behave like mix of the other two. It has generally been accepted that the
variable speed (frequency control) can provide energy savings for part load.
Figure 3.10 shows how the UA and the corresponding fan power ˙W changes,
when the fan capacity changes [18]. For the example shown in Figure 3.10 for
50% of UA100 one can achieve 82% power savings when choosing the variable
speed control. Numbers can be seen in Table 3.7.
Table 3.7: Example of power saving by throttling capacity at dry coolers.

==PAGE:47==
3.3. STORAGE
33
3.3
Storage
3.3.1
General
The thermal energy storage is able to store heating or cooling for later use. The
main feature of storage is to correct for mismatch between supply and demand
or postpone the use of high power consuming units like the vapor compression
cycle. An example of a mismatch is if the cooling system cannot provide the
cooling needed, then can the storage supplement with the remaining. Storage
often used to handle peak load [26]. It is possible to beneﬁts of oﬀ-peak electric
tariﬀs.
Chillers and dry coolers run full capacity during night time, when
temperature and power prices are low, charging the storage. The cold ambient
temperature lowers the condensing temperature, thus the COP increases.
Implementing storage capacity one can observe [26, 27]:
• Reduced costs both capital investment and operational.
• Reduced power consumption.
• Increased ﬂexibility in production/operation.
• Reduced equipment size / better utilization of equipment.
• Reduced emissions.
The storage procedure includes three steps: Charging, storing and discharging.
A heat transfer ﬂuid is circulated between the cooling producing unit and the
storage. The heat transfer ﬂuid and the storing material can either be the same
or two diﬀerent. One should look for high energy density in storage material
and good heat transfer between the heat transfer ﬂuid and storage material. It
is possible to water as media for both which can eliminate a heat exchanger.
Furthermore one should strive for easy control and limitation of heat loss by
insulating the storage (or gain if the ambient temperature is above the storage
temperature). It is possible to store thermal energy in diﬀerent ways.
Sensible energy is stored when the temperature of the storage medium in-
creases or decreases, thus temperature changes. The typical storage media are
water or granite. These materials have a high energy density (ρ · cp) as both
the density and speciﬁc heat are relatively large.
Latent heat uses the phase change of a heat storage medium. Typically it
is the phase change between solid and liquid. The latent heat needed for the
transition makes it possible to store large amounts of energy at constant tem-
perature. Heat must be removed from the storage for the media to solidify.
Media for this type of storage are called PCM (phase changing material) and
can be water/ice, paraﬃn, salt or salt-hydrates.
Thermochemical energy storage relies on reversible chemical reactions with
high energy involved. The products of a reaction should be able to be sepa-
rated during the process and storage separately. When mixing the products
the process should be reversed. These processes typically use or release some
heat rate to happen. This heat rate comes from the cooling system. When
==PAGE:48==
34
CHAPTER 3. THERMODYNAMIC METHOD
discharging the storage the reaction absorbs energy from the cooling water.
The choice or selection of technology depends on storage period required, eco-
nomic viability, operational conditions etc.
3.3.2
Sensible Thermal Energy Storage
In this project water is chosen both as the heat transfer ﬂuid and the storage
media. Water is easily transported by pumps and provide a good heat transfer
coeﬃcient. Long term seasonal storage are possible if the heat storage volume
is large [28]. Sensible water storage is 6-7 times larger than an ice storage with
same capacity but is 20% cheaper [26]. The operation costs are expected to
be lower for sensible due to the higher evaporation temperature in the VCC,
which leads to a lower COP.
Four diﬀerent designs to store energy using water as medium presented by
Zontag [29] and can be seen in Figure 3.11. In general the concept is to have a
cold region from which for example water can be used to supply cooling, and a
hot region for the warm water returning from the data center. Common for all
types is that the storage can be charged during cold periods, where the cooling
system can provide an excess of cooling compared to the demand. The cold
water can be discharged as needed when the cooling system is unable to meet
the cooling demand. The charge and discharge can either be from day to day
using the lower night temperature or seasonal, utilizing the cold winter climate
to provide cooling during the warmer summer months.
(a) Aquifer storage
(b) Borehole storage
(c) Tank storage
(d) Pit storage
Figure 3.11: Sensible Thermal Energy Storage [29].
The aquifer thermal energy storage (ATES) can be designed in various ways
depending on the available conditions. The ATES seen on Figure 3.11(a) utilize
==PAGE:49==
3.3. STORAGE
35
two separated underground water reservoirs to store heating or cooling. Each
reservoir consist of a permeable material, such as sand, gravel and sandstone,
within a shell or boundary is a more impermeable material like rock or clay
[26]. Both the water and the permeable material acts as the storage medium.
With regard to cooling, the system is charged by pumping water from the warm
reservoir, cooling it via heat exchange from surplus capacity and pumping it
to the cold reservoir. The discharging is the reversed operation, pumping cold
water from the cold reservoir, utilizing the cooling and discharging to the warm
reservoir.
Borehole thermal energy storage (BTES) consists of multiple small holes drilled
into the rock or soil ground in a circular pattern. Using U-pipes, each hole is
connected from the center to the edges. To charge the system for heating, hot
water supplied by for example solar heating is pumped into the center pipes,
slowly heating the surrounding soil and rock, as seen on Figure 3.11(b), causing
the center of the heat up. When heat is required during winter, cold water is
pumped in the opposite direction, from the edges to the center, drawing heat
from the soil and rock to the water. The BTES can furthermore be operated
for cooling storage, simply by supplying a cold working medium to the center
of the borehole system [30]. The disadvantage of the borehole system is a sig-
niﬁcantly lower heat capacity per volume compared to water.
The most commonly used type of thermal energy storage is a thermally strati-
ﬁed tank. The tank exploits the simple fact that hot water has a lower density
than cold water, causing hot water to ﬂoat to the top and cold water to sink
in the tank, which can be seen on Figure 3.11(c). Depending on whether heat-
ing or cooling is desired, water is discharged either at the top or the bottom.
When charging it is important to avoid mixing as much as possible, maintain-
ing the stratiﬁcation, by careful placement of in- and outlets. The pit storage
is an extension of the tank storage concept, but instead of a steel tank, a pit is
dug, insulated and covered with a membrane, as seen on ﬁgure Figure 3.11(d).
Apart from the diﬀerent design, the pit operates mostly as the steel tanks.
The pit uses the natural insulation of the dirt to save material, compared to
the steel tank will require full insulation. Both types require more insulation
measures compared to the borehole and aquifer systems, to avoid heat loss or
ingress for heating and cooling storage respectively.
As the geophysics plays a signiﬁcant role for the operation of the aquifer and
borehole solutions, these are omitted. The steel tanks and pit storage are likely
choices, as the modeling and behavior are similar.
==PAGE:50==
36
CHAPTER 3. THERMODYNAMIC METHOD
3.3.3
Equations in model
The physical layout of the storage are not considered. The storage are assumed
perfectly insulated such that no heat transfer from the water to the surround-
ings.
The storage is modeled with assuming two volumes: A hot and a cold, T1 and
T3 respectively. The volumes are separated with an inﬁnitesimal thermocline.
No mixing and heat transfer happen between the two regions. One could say
it is perfect stratiﬁed.
The modeling is based on the cold volume, which acts like the master. The
hot volume are the slave following VST = VST,h + VST,c. Volume added in
one region equals removed volume in the other region. The volume of the cold
water can be in the range of empty to completely full. Empty would lead to
the total tank volume has the temperature T1. The tank temperature is T3
when the tank ’full’.
0m3 ≤VST,c ≤VST
(3.36)

mass inﬂow rate charging or mass outﬂow rate discharging.

3.3.4
Storage time
The time it takes to discharge a full storage tank can be calculated as the total
volume divided by the need volume ﬂow rate of cooling water. The volume
ﬂow rate is constant for each hour and is determined by the temperature set
(T1/T3) and the cooling demand ( ˙Qd).
τ = VST,C
˙Vw,d
(3.46)
The discharge time is linear as the volume ﬂow rate is constant. Figure 3.13
show how long time a storage at a given size can handle the complete cooling
demand alone. Due to the relatively high volume ﬂow rate caused by the 10
MW cooling demand large storage volumes are needed for long term storage,
seasonal storage. The graph does also reveal that the cold water temperature
diﬀerence plays a great role. Having ∆T = 5 K versus ∆T = 15 K decreases
the discharge time by a factor three - the storage need to be three times large
to have the same discharge time. This is directly given from the formula (3.47).
The density and speciﬁc heat capacity changes slightly with increasing target
temperature, however the does not impact signiﬁcantly. The storage time is
approximately the same for all temperature levels as long as the ∆T is the
same.
˙Qd = ˙Vw,d · ρw · cp,w · (T1 −T3)
(3.47)
The need for a ∆T = 15 K is approximately 576 m3/h.

Chapter 4
Economic valuation method
4.1
Introduction
In the process of valuating and choosing the most feasible among diﬀerent
projects, here cooling plants, one can venture to go for the lowest total capital
investment (TCI), however it might no be the true optimal solution. It is im-
portant also to include the income and expenses during the complete technical
lifetime, n. The technical lifetime is the expected time for the equipment to be
functional. Here assumed to be 20 years.
The Net Present Value method is able to include both present and future in-
comes and expenses in a single value - the value of the total project today
include time value of money [31, 32]. The NPV is deﬁned as the sum of all
discounted net cash ﬂows in the lifetime and is typically evaluated on annual
basis.
NPV0 =
n
X
z=0
CFnet,z(1 + r)n
(4.1)
Where r is the discount rate. A discount rate is dissatisﬁed one is of the future.
The value should equal the company opportunity cost of capital, which is the
expected rate on return on an investor could get in an other investment with
similar risk. The speciﬁc value can be hard to identify, why it in this analysis
is assume to be 7%. A sensitivity analysis is made instead, see section 4.5.
The net cash ﬂow is given by equation (4.2), which should be evaluate n times.
The unit is M’DKK/yr. The net cash ﬂow is the sum of the annual investments,
working capital, revenues, expenses and taxes.
CFnet = CFinvest + CFW C + CF revenues + CFexpenses + CF taxes
(4.2)
The investment typically happens in year 0. The same year one set working
capital a side to have money to sustain the operation expenses - in principle
just money on a bank account. The cooling systems in this project is assumed
to be owned by the data center, which means that the there is not product to
sell (e.g. cooling) and therefore no revenues. A consequence of this is that the
tax cash ﬂow always is zero. Taxes are only paid on earnings [32]. The cash
39

in-ﬂows are positive and cash out-ﬂows are negative.
Table 4.1 shows the cash ﬂows used to evaluate the present value of the diﬀerent
system designs in this project. It can be seen that for all years the net cash ﬂow
is negative, which makes the NPV negative. In normal investment decision
making the investment yielding the highest NPV is the way to go. Investments
with a negative NPV would be rejected. They are a bad investments. But in
this case where the data center critically needs a cooling system, there is really
no choice. One should still go for the system design that has the highest NPV
- even though it is negative.

4.2
Operational and maintenance costs
The operational and maintenance costs covers all cash ﬂows related to the daily
operation of the cooling system. It can be the costs for power purchase, labor
salary, unforeseen break-downs, general maintenance (lubrication, sealing etc.).
The amount of labors is most likely the same independent of the system layout
and size. Adding more storage, vapor compression cycles or heat exchangers
does not increase the need for manual labor, thus neglected. The unforeseen
break-downs is also neglected.

The thermal model provide the annual power consumption for the cooling
system.

when multiplying with an annual average power price.

the forecast for the spot price. From 2025-2035 the price are based on calcu-
lations. The power price has a compounded annual growth rate of 3.13% from
2015-2035. Figure 4.1 shows the data the the corresponding trend line. The
price starts at round 476 DKK/MWh and ends at 882 DKK/MWh.
DONG Energy’s energy management advice department for large scale con-
sumers (>2 GWh/yr) states1 that the power price for a data center could be
in the range from 500-900 DKK/MWh depending on the location, if a new
generator station is needed, if it is close to transmission lines or not. They
advised to use 750 DKK/MWh. Both 750 and 900 DKK/MWh in 2016 for
extrapolated using the same annual growth rate as the socio-economical (Pri-
vate1 and Private2). These are higher due to added taxes and transmission
fees.

4.3
Total capital investment
The ’Total Capital Investment’ (TCI) is the capital that must be paid at time
zero, when the design and construction starts. Some times it is called Capital
Expenditures (CAPEX). The investment of the cooling system is more than
just the costs of the storage, vapor compression cycle and dry coolers. The cost
does also among others include capital for buying land, building the necessary
facilities and do the installation. Bejan et al. [31] presents a general list of costs
connected to the total capital investment. The list is divided into two part;
’Fixed Capital Investment’ (FCI) and ’Other Outlays’ (OO).
TCI = FCI + OO
(4.8)
The FCI includes all the ’Direct Costs’ (DC). DC is the sum of the pur-
chased equipment, which is the main components, and installation, piping,
instrumentation, control, other electrical equipment and service facilities are
in the category. Buying the land and doing civil and structural changes are
signiﬁcant in this context as the dry coolers need a large ground area. Even
more signiﬁcant is the structural costs for removing soil for the pit storage.
FCI does also include the indirect costs, which is costs for engineering, con-
struction and contingencies.
Other outlays, OO, are start-up costs, working capital, costs of licensing and re-
search and development. Allowances for funds used during construction might
be an outlay.
In general it is diﬃcult to state the costs of the individual points without get-
ting an oﬀer from a contractor, however Bejan et al. [31] provides percentage
intervals related to the ’Purchased Equipment Cost’ (PEC) which can give an
estimation of the individual costs, see Table 4.3. The purchased equipment cost
is sum of the main components costs, which is estimated in section 4.4. The
percentage intervals are rather large supporting the uncertainties associated.
The values used in Table 4.3 are average or recommended values which can be
==PAGE:57==

used in absence of more accurate numbers [31].

that the percentages are equal for all the cooling systems investigated in this
thesis, one can use this simple estimate the total capital investment,
TCI = 6.1 · PEC
(4.9)
Similar can the working capital (WC) be estimated as,
WC = 0.53 · PEC
(4.10)

4.4
Component costs
Estimating the costs for the main components can be done in more ways. The
best and most accurate method is to ask for vendor/contractor quotation, but
it can be a slow process. The next best option is to use references to previous
similar project, where the prices are known. In lack of previous projects and
when time is important, one can turn into estimating charts from the litera-
ture.
Cost estimating charts correlate the price of a given technology with its size.
They have been obtained through the correlation of a large number of costs
and design data. Typically is the correlation between equipment size and costs
linear when plotted in a log-log plot. For the storage the size is install vol-
umen VST [m3], the vapor compression cycle the size is the maximum useful
cooling rate ˙QE [kW]. For the dry coolers it is the heat transfer capacitance
UA [kW/K].
The gradient of the line, α, is an important scaling parameter. The cost of a
component can be scaled with equation (4.11). The cost of a reference compo-
nent is multiplied with the fraction of the new size to the reference size. The
fraction is in the power of α. A correction factor, F, can be necessary to use
depending on the literature.
Costnew = F · Costref ·
sizenew
sizeref
α
(4.11)
Woods [34] made a comprehensive list of capital cost guidelines for process
equipment. Size and costs of reference components including the scaling expo-
nent and correction factors are available for a large variety technologies. The
costs are for the unit itself but not the infrastructure (piping, cabling etc.).
4.4.1
Vapor compression cycle
Data for the reference VCC can be seen in Table 4.4. The correlation is only
valid for installed capacity be 20-5000 kW. For this reason the total installed
capacity must be divided into a number of smaller vapor compression cycles.
This corresponds well to the assumption of ability to run partial load even from
very small cooling demands. The guess value of the size is 1000 kW, a value
arbitrarily chosen within the valid range.

4.4.3
Storage
The storage costs is subject to large uncertainties. The magnitude of required
volume is very large compared to the general heat storage. As discussed in
subsection 3.3.2 more options on sensible heat storage is available.
Pit heat storage is the most feasible for very large volumes in Danish context
due to the speciﬁc storage cost (DKK/m3) [35]. Prior to 2011 was 10, 000m3
the maximum volume in operation, since then has a 60, 000m3 in Dronninglund
and 75, 000m3 in Marstal been build. In 2015 was the worlds largest pit heat
storage inaugurated. The pit is 205, 000m3 and placed in Vojens.
==PAGE:62==
48
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC VALUATION METHOD
The literature is not very clear on the cost scaling as the pit storage is a rather
new in this size. Not enough data is available to make good cost estimating
charts, further reasons are design diﬀerences and the uncertainties associated
with the excavation costs. The soil and underground can impact the costs a
lot [36].
The investment for the storage in Vojens was 33.8 M’DKK [37].
It is not
speciﬁed what is included in the price. Assuming that it is the total capital
investment TCI brings the purchased equipment cost down to 5.54 M’DKK
using the TCI = 6.1 · PEC relation. The older literature tries to estimate the
cost of a 200, 000m3 at that times basis. The cost span is large from 3.2-5.4
M’DKK [35] and 6.75 M’DKK [38]. The literature does a better match on the
price of a 75, 000m3 storage. The costs are 2.34 M’DKK [35], 3.32 M’DKK [38]
and 3.79 M’DKK [36].
Figure 4.5 shows the cost based on the actual price for Vojens and a scaling
exponent 0.7. The costs estimated in the literature are plotted in the same
graph. The curve ﬁt decent to the data for the Marstal size. The costs for steel
tanks are also plotted. The cost estimation for the steel tanks are only valid
up to 40, 000 m3, thus must the total installed storage volume, VST , be divided
into a number of smaller tanks within the valid range. A single steel tank at
40, 000 m3 costs 13.88 M’DKK. Buying 1.5 · 106 m3 (38 tanks) costs staggering
523 M’DKK. The reference storage data is in Table 4.8.

4.5. SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS
49
4.5
Sensitivity analysis
The economic key parameters in the NPV analysis are values assumed values,
why a sensitivity analysis can show the inﬂuence on the present value of a
project. The case used as reference:
• System 1, 25/10 ◦C
• Dry cooler: UA100 = 3000 kW/K
• Storage: VST = 375000 m3
• VCC capacity: QE,max = 10000 kW
The calculation using the parameters shown in Table 4.2 yield a present value
of this particular setup of -499.5 M’DKK. This is the base. By changing the
lifetime, discount rate, growth rate, total capital investment (TCI) and the
power price one at the time, the inﬂuence on that particular parameter can be
investigated. The other parameters are kept at base value.
Figure 4.6 shows the relative change in in present value as function of the
relative parameter change. It is clear from the ﬁgure that the total capital
investment has the biggest impact on the NPV calculation. Increasing the
investment 25% decreases the present value 22%.
The lifetime parameter in the range 10 to 30 years gives changes from 4.55%
to -2.8. The discount rate in the range 3.5-10.5% changes the present value
±0.65%. Increasing the discount rate decreases increases the PV . The opposite
trend is spotted for the power price growth rate. Increasing the rate results in
lower PV . A variation from 1.6-4.7% give present value changes from 0.75% to
-0.89%. The power price is varied from 375-1125 DKK/MWh and has almost
similar importance as the lifetime but still the changes in PV low ±3.23%.
The conclusion is that a study of the actual investment is of much higher
importance than the discussion on estimated lifetime and power price. The
discount and growth rate are not of signiﬁcant importance.
Of cause it is
optimal to use the true values for all the parameters.

5.1
Concept of operational control scheme
5.1.1
Temperature limit
There is an upper limit, Tlim, for the ambient temperature for which the FC
is able to provide exactly the required cooling to satisfy the demand, ˙Qd. Tlim
is completely depended on the UA-value of the FC. Solving the three energy
equations (3.15)(3.16)(3.21), the ambient temperature limit can be found - as
well as the air outlet temperature. The fans are running at 100% capacity.
Tlim will always be lower than the water target temperature T3, see Figure 5.1.
T
Tlim
T3
T1
Figure 5.1: Key temperatures along temperature axis
It is desirable to dimension the system for a high Tlim, atleast when the eco-
nomics are left out, as the number of hours where the FC can provide suﬃcient
cooling by it self increases. The ambient temperature distribution of the Copen-
hagen area is shown in Figure 5.2. The temperatures are symmetric bimodal
distributed which means that two local optima is present. One around 1−3 ◦C
and another at 11−13 ◦C. The distribution is approximately symmetric around
the average temperature 7.76 ◦C. From this it is clear that just a small increase
in Tlim (i.e. increase in UA or T3) can increase the annual FC share signif-
icantly for ambient temperatures in the range −1 ◦C to 17 ◦C, as 78.9% of
the hours in the year is in this interval. The occurrence of these temperatures
are high as they are present in all seasons of the year. The tails in both ends
are due to the summer and winter season, where it is either relatively warm
or cold. These seasons also provide temperatures in the upper or lower part of
the middle section, respectively, thus the two optima.
Figure 5.3 shows the correlation of the heat exchanger size (e.g. UA) and the
ambient temperature limit for which the FC is able to cover the full cooling
demand. For temperatures below the limit the FC has an overcapacity and the
fans must be throttled or the excess cooling stored for later use. For tempera-
tures above Tlim the system needs additional cooling from a VCC or a thermal

It is desirable to have the highest Tlim possible as the need for additional
cooling is decreased. It can be seen that high temperature diﬀerences on the
waterside, ∆Tw = 15 K, results in higher Tlim compared to ∆Tw = 5 K. The
reason for the higher limiting ambient temperature (air inlet) can be directly
found in the energy equations. The logarithmic mean temperature diﬀerence
should be the same for both cases of ∆Tw, if the heat transfer capacitance, UA,
as well as the cooling demand, ˙Qd, are the same. This means that changes in
water temperature inﬂuence the air temperatures.
The advantage for the high ∆T decreases as the UA increases and the ambient
limit temperature reaches the target temperature T3 = {10, 20, 30}◦C.
The graph does also reveal that a higher water diﬀerence temperature can
==PAGE:67==
5.1. CONCEPT OF OPERATIONAL CONTROL SCHEME
53
decrease the heat transfer area signiﬁcantly if a given Tlim is required. When
Tlim →T3 the “UA savings” increases. The eﬀects for diﬀerent heat exchanger
sizes can be seen in Table 5.1.
Example
The system has a target temperature T3 = 10 ◦C and the ∆Tw =
5 K. The installed UA = 3000 kW/K provides a Tlim of approximately 6.5 ◦C.
If one allowed a change in water temperature diﬀerence to 15 K one could
decrease the installed UA to 1700 kW/K.
