5.1.2
Possible combinations
The system conﬁguration has the three components: Free cooler, vapor com-
pression cycle and storage. The components can either be activated or deac-
tivated, this results in four bits as the storage both are able to charge (+)
and discharge (÷). Four bits can provide 42 = 16 signals. The signals are
converted into operational states of which some are possible and some are not
from a practical point of view. The operational states are shown in Table 5.2.
The states that are impossible or excluded from the simple modeling are marked
in gray. In state 9 none of the components are active meaning that no cool-
ing is produced or drawn from the thermal storage. This are not a possible
solution as the cooling demand is assumed to be 10 MW continuously. State
10-14 dictates simultaneous charging and discharging of the storage, this can
not happen. In state 14 the only active part is storage charging, but there is
nothing active to provide the cooling, thus impossible. In the initial simula-
tions the VCC are not allowed to charge the storage and can therefore not be
active in same states as storage charging is active. State 15-16 are not allowed.
Looking at the states that actually are possible, the only state where the stor-
age is able to charge is state 1. Here is the FC active and are able to produce
more cooling than needed. The mass ﬂow rate through the FC are higher than
the demand ˙mw > ˙mw,d. The excess mass ﬂow rate are transferred to the
storage, in which the cold volume displaces the warm. It is always prioritized
to charge the storage as much as possible, which means that the fan speed at
the dry coolers should be 100%. However if the storage are chosen to be a
limited size and not inﬁnitely large, one could end up in a situation, where
the FC are able to exceed the available storage volume. In these cases the fan
speed are throttled to match the remaining storage volume in the speciﬁc hour
(state 1b).
In general the FC fans are running at 100% in order to maximize the heat
==PAGE:68==
54
CHAPTER 5. MODELING CONCEPT AND DATA FLOW
transfer from cooling water to the ambient at all times.
In state 2 the cooling demand is covered by the FC alone. This requires that
the UA-value is large enough to provide the needed cooling for a given ambient
temperature and that Tamb ≤T3. If the FC is assumed to act alone with no
interaction with VCC or ST, then the mass ﬂow rate ˙mw,F C = ˙mw,d and the
temperature diﬀerence ∆Tw,F C = T1 −T3.
Operational state 5 is excluded from the simulations even though it is possible,
more on that topic in the discussion in chapter 11.

5.1.3
Modeling the control system
The water temperatures, T1 and T3, are used to control the ﬂow in the system,
while the ambient temperature and the cold storage volume, VST,c, are used to
control how the cooling system should operate.
The concept of the control algorithm is based on the same temperature axis as
shown in Figure 5.1. The axis does now represent the ambient temperature.
The known temperatures Tlim, T3 and T1 are marks, that split the ambient
temperature range into four parts. Each of the four parts have its own priori-
==PAGE:69==
5.1. CONCEPT OF OPERATIONAL CONTROL SCHEME
55
tized list of actions, shown in Figure 5.4.
The decision algorithm is made with a series of nested if-statement. The outer
if-statements dictates which part of the algorithm should be entered using the
ambient temperature. For an example see Appendix K. The ambient temper-
ature is easy to measure, why it often is included in control algorithms. The
inner if-statements specify which operational state should be used in that par-
ticular hour according to the deﬁned priority list of actions. It will depend on
both how the dry coolers perform at that ambient temperature and and the
cold water level in the storage.
The general priority of the control system for the three systems are:
1. Always charge storage if possible.
2. Always use as much free cooling as possible.
3. Always use storage before vapor compression cycle.
The operational states for ambient temperatures below Tlim are the same for
the three systems. The systems act like a setup with dry coolers and storage
only. It is possible to free cool more than the cooling demand due to the def-
inition. The ﬁrst priority is always to charge the storage as much as possible
when the dry coolers have overcapacity. State 1a maximizes the capacity of
the dry coolers by running the fans at 100%. When the storage is close to full
or already are full, the fans are throttled to match maximum charge mass ﬂow
rate corresponding to state 1b .
For ambient temperatures above T1 the algorithm also have identical control
schemes. When the ambient temperatures is above the water return tempera-
ture is it not possible to use dry coolers. Leading the water through the heat
exchargers would result in a water temperature increase which is not desirable.
The ﬁrst priority is to use cold water from the storage if available, state
4 . If
the water level in the storage does not meet a full hour demand, the storage will
be supplemented by the VCC, this is state
7 . The very last in the priority,
state
8
is used if the storage is empty.
What happens between Tlim and T1 is system speciﬁc and will be described in
later sections.
5.1.4
Forced storage discharge
In order to be able to compare the results from the numerous simulations one
must make sure that the storage are at the same charge level as in the beginning
of the simulation, which here is zero cold water available. The storage must
be forced to discharge at the end of the simulation. FC and VCC can then be
turned oﬀin these hours saving power. It makes good sense as these hours are
some of the warmest.
Based on the cooling water temperatures and the continuous cooling demand
of 10 MW the needed volume ﬂow rate, ˙Vw,d, is known and constant for each
hour. The time the storage can be only source of cooling can be calculated

The forced end-of-year discharge is implemented as a if-statement, algorithm

possible, if the storage is able to deliver a full hour of cooling.

3: else
4:

5: end if
==PAGE:71==
5.2. GENERAL ASSUMPTIONS
57
5.2
General assumptions
Below is a list of assumptions that is used for the simulations of the data center
cooling system. Most of them are used to simplify the models.
• Cooling demand is constant and continuous ˙Qd = 10 MW.
• VCC not allowed to charge storage.
• VCC dimensioned to handle the cooling demand alone at all times.
• VCC can cope with all operational conditions. Partial load 0-100%.
• Thermal storage consist of a cold and a warm volume which sums up to
the total volume. The volumes are separated with a inﬁnitesimal thin
and well insulated plate.
• Uniform temperature T1 and T3 in storage volumes with no internal heat
transfer. The water will not be subject to Buoyancy ﬂow.
• No heat loss and pressure drops in the system.
• Hourly based simulations (quasi static). Variables time frame are seconds
and assumed to be constant for 3600 s.
• In general the FC fans are running at 100% in order to maximize the
heat transfer from cooling water to the ambient at all times.
• Simulations start 1. October.
• Storage starts with VST,c = 0 m3
• UA from 0-8000 kW/K in increments of 1000 kW/K
• Storage volume from 0m3−1.5 · 106 m3 in increments of 187, 500 m3. Stor-
age time up to approximately 100 days.
• No limits on charging and discharging mass ﬂow rates.
• Valves and other control mechanisms are not considered.
• Pumps for storage loop in neglected due to the assumption of no pressure
loss
• Storage can not be ’overcharged’. Water leaving the storage when charg-
ing must be T1.
==PAGE:72==
58
CHAPTER 5. MODELING CONCEPT AND DATA FLOW
5.3
Data ﬂow
5.3.1
Overall
The overall data ﬂow is illustrated in Figure 5.6. The heart of the data ﬂow is
a number of Excel ﬁles containing the model inputs and results.
The ﬁrst step is to specify the inputs in the excel sheets. The inputs are used to
quantify temperatures, demand and dimensions. See section 5.3.2 for further
details.
When the input parameters are written for all the planned simulations, the
thermal model can start. The simulations are started by activating an EES
macro. The macro reads input parameters for a single run from Excel and
use them in an EES model for a full year simulation. When the simulation is
done, the results are exported to the same Excel sheet as the input parameters.
The macro reads new inputs for another run and starts a new simulation. The
macro loops through all planned simulations. More on the macro in section
5.3.3.
After the thermodynamic simulations are done, one can start preparing the
economic valuation. A simple data handling program extract the important
data such as power consumption from the large number of Excel ﬁles. With
the extracted data can the economic valuation be made.
5.3.2
Input/Results documents
Each of the Excel ﬁles has nine sheets, which all represent a system conﬁgu-
ration. The layout of the sheets are identical. The ﬁrst columns in the sheets
are the inputs for simulation models. These numbers quantify dimensions and
temperatures. After those columns follow columns where the results from the
simulations are inserted.
Inputs:
Speciﬁed in the ﬁrst 11 columns. The hour parameter runs from
1 to 8760 in increments of one - corresponding to the time resolution of the
simulations.
The next columns are the parameters associated with the va-
por compression cycle (∆TEtoCmin, ∆TC, ∆Tp,E and ηCt). The installed heat
transfer capacitance, UA100, and volume of the storage, VST are also speciﬁed
together with the water temperatures deﬁning the system (T1 and T3). The
last inputs are the cooling demand, ˙Qd, and the ambient temperature from the
DRY data. The ﬁrst hour correspond of the 0:30 1st October.
Results:
Speciﬁed in the next 13 columns. The cold volume of the storage
tank is exported, thus the time variation can be plotted. A column follows with
the actual operational states for the particular hours. The heat transfer rates
in the dry coolers, vapor compression cycle and storage charge and discharge
( ˙QF C, ˙QV CC, ˙Qcharge and ˙Qdischarge) are followed by the power consumption
of the dry cooler and VCC ( ˙WF C and ˙WV CC). Finally the actual UA for the
particular hour and limit temperature, air outlet temperature and the middle
temperature T2 for the series systems are saved.

CHAPTER 5. MODELING CONCEPT AND DATA FLOW
The ﬁles are structure such that all conﬁgurations of a single system layout
(1,2 or 3) at a given temperature set (T1/T3) are placed in the same folder.
An example is shown in Table 5.3. System 1 at the temperature set 15/10 ◦C
has nine Excel ﬁles with nine sheets each. Such a folder includes 81 full year
simulations. The number of system layouts and temperature sets add up to a
total of 18 folders and 1458 full year simulations.

5.3.3
EES macro
The macro running all the thermodynamic simulations are made in the simu-
lation software, EES, using the build-in tools for macros. The purpose of using
a macro is to automatize the process of running the nearly 1500 simulations.
A small section of the macro is shown in algorithm 2. First the directory is
assigned to a string variable Dir$, using the build-in function GetDirectory.
Having the ﬁle path to the directory assigned to a variable is convenient later
in the macro, when the respective Excel and EES ﬁles are called. The macro
can handle if the ﬁles are moved to another directory as long as the macro ﬁle
is in the same folder.
Afterwards the EES ﬁle with the model is assigned to a variable and opened.
The || is the syntax for combining two strings. After that an Excel document
is assigned to the variable, F1$. It is smart to do the assignment as the same
ﬁles are referred to more than once. If the ﬁle names are changed only few
places in the macro need modiﬁcation.
The macro opens the Excel ﬁle F1$ and enters sheet ’R1’. An array with all
the inputs for the model are copied and pasted into a parametric table in EES.
The table is solved, while updating guess values. An option for simulation stop
is added if a particular hour can not be solved. When the full year simulation
is complete the results, an array, is copied to the same Excel sheet. The ﬁle is
saved and a 2 second break starts.
After the pause the macro enters the next sheet in F1$ and follows the same
routine is described above. Here follows the remaining sheets in the Excel ﬁle
(sheets ’R1’ to ’R9’). When one Excel ﬁle is done the macro loops to the
next and the next, until all are done. The macro ends by prompting a pop-up
window stating that the simulation is done.

5.3.4
Data handling program
A small program has been made in C# to ease the data handling by automa-
tizing to the picking of important parameters.
The program looks in the folder where all the subfolder including the Excel
ﬁles are. The subfolders are as shown in Table 5.3. The names of all subfolders
are listed for looping. Afterwards starts a nested loop over the single folders
and the ﬁles within. For each ﬁle single cells are picked and simple actions like
summation and ﬁnding maximum are performed before inserting the values in
a Excel document called ’Results.xlsx’.

==PAGE:77==
Chapter 6
System 0, VCC (base case)
6.1
System description
The reference system is a traditional chiller based on a vapor compression cycle.
The water from the data center enters the evaporator with temperature, T1, at
a mass ﬂow rate ˙mw,d corresponding to the thermal load. The water exits the
evaporator at the desired target temperature, T3. Figure 6.1 shows a simple
drawing of the layout. Only one single stage VCC is drawn, however in practice
the system would either a number of parallel VCCs or a system with more than
one compressor in parallel. The assumption for the vapor compression cycles
in the simulations is the ability to run partial load from 0-100% at all times.
The system does not include any dry coolers or storage options. The system
follows the equations for VCC as described in section 3.1.
˙mw,d
T1
˙mw,d
T3

Figure 6.1: Layout of system 0.
6.2
Operational states and control
The simulation of system 0 is straight forward. No special operational states
between technologies are required. The vapor compression cycle has to remove
the full thermal load, ˙Qd, at all times cooling the water from T1 to T3.
The control scheme is shown in Figure 6.2. For all ambient temperatures state
8
is the only options, which according to Table 5.2 is when the VCC is the
only active component.

6.3
Results
The power consumption of system 0 is only inﬂuenced by the cold water target
temperature, T3, due to the deﬁnition of the evaporation temperature. The
evaporator is not limited by a physical size (UA) and is there not inﬂuenced
by the water temperature returning from the data center, T1. Changing T1
would change water ﬂow rate, but as no pressure losses are taken into account
and the additional pump work is neglected. Table 6.1 shows the annual power
consumption for the three target temperatures considered in the case. The
average COP along with the maximum and minimum COP are included. One
observe a decreasing power consumption as the target temperature increases.
The increase in temperature result in slightly increase in annual average COP
from 8.152 for T3 = 10 ◦C to 8.945 for T3 = 30 ◦C. This is primarily due to
the deﬁnition of COPC. The higher the target temperature is, the closer the
average temperature is to the maximum COP - but in general the average
COP is rather high for the all the temperatures.

Chapter 7
System 1, Series FC and VCC
7.1
System description
System 1 is a series setup where the water returning from the data center ﬁrst
enters the dry coolers and then the evaporator of the vapor compression cycle.
The thermal storage is parallel to this ’cooling string’. The water returns from
the data center with temperature T1.
After the dry coolers the water has
temperature T2. If it is not possible to use free cooling then T2 = T1. The
VCC brings the temperature from T2 to T3.
The storage can be charged if the dry coolers have overcapacity, which requires
that the ambient temperature T11 < Tlim. The dry coolers are able to bring
the temperature to T3 and at a mass ﬂow rate higher than what the data center
requires, ˙mw > ˙mw,d. In that case the additional mass ﬂow rate is induced by
the storage loop, ˙mw = ˙mST + ˙mw,d. The water circulates clockwise, while the
water ﬂow ˙mw,d to the data center is maintained.
In a similar way it is possible to discharge the storage if needed. The mass ﬂow
rate is split such that ˙mw,d = ˙mST + ˙mw.
˙mw,d
T1
˙mw
T2
˙mw,d
T3
T1
T3
˙mST
˙mST
T11
T12
˙ma
Figure 7.1: Layout of system 1
65
==PAGE:80==
66
CHAPTER 7. SYSTEM 1, SERIES FC AND VCC
7.2
System speciﬁc assumptions
The ﬂow direction of the ’cooling string’ can only be from left to right.
T1 →T2 →T3
In practice a check valve would be placed after the vapor compression cycle.
The ’cooling string’ in the cycle layout in Figure 7.1 is drawn such that the
water ﬂow is through both the dry cooler and the vapor compression cycle at all
times. In principle one would observe a heat ingress for ambient temperature
above T1 in the dry cooler. In practice it should be possible to by-pass the dry
cooler, otherwise the temperature T2 could end up being higher than T1. In
the simulations full by-pass is assumed.
7.3
Operational states and control
The operational control scheme is presented in Figure 7.2. The numbers refer
to the state numbers in Table 5.2.
For ambient temperatures below the limit temperature, the ambient tempera-
ture for which the dry coolers just exactly are able to manage the full cooling
demand, system 1 follows what is described in section 5.1.3. State 1a maxi-
mizes the capacity of the dry coolers and storage charging by running the fans
at 100%. When the storage is close to full or already are full, are the fans
throttled to match maximum charge mass ﬂow rate corresponding to state 1b .
When the ambient temperature exceed Tlim, the dry coolers can not be the only
source of cooling. As long as Tamb is below T3 it is possible to cool a smaller
mass ﬂow rate to the target temperature. The rest of the cooling should the
be provided by the storage. This option is state
3
and should be prioritized.
If the storage can not provide the needed mass ﬂow rate one has to use a com-
bination of FC and VCC - called state
6 . The dry coolers runs at 100% fan
speed and lower the water temperature at ˙mw = ˙mw,d as much as possible.
The VCC provides the remaining cooling and brings the temperature from T2
to T3.
Those hours where the ambient temperature are in the interval [T3 ; T1[ it is
not possible for the dry coolers to cool the water to the target temperature at
all, thus is state
6
needed again. Dry coolers with 100% fan speed cool the
water to T2, which is ≥Tamb. The VCC brings the temperature from T2 to
T3. If the storage is able to provide cooling for a full hour is this option ﬁrst
priority. The option is state
4 .
Similar to the other system layouts for Tamb > T1 the ﬁrst priority is to use
cold water from the storage if available, state
4 . If the water level in the
storage does not meet a full hour demand, the storage will be supplemented by
the VCC, this is state
7 . The very last in the priority list, state
8
is used
if the storage is empty.
State
5
are marked in the control scheme, however not an option integrated
into the simulation model. The option is excluded as it requires some kind of

The option would likely be used for all ambient temperatures between Tlim and

7.4
Model test and veriﬁcation
7.4.1
Static test
In order to verify that the EES model behaves according to the control scheme
ﬁve scenarios has been investigated. The goal is to see if the decision algorithm
chooses the right states for diﬀerent ambient temperatures and with diﬀerent
charge levels in the storage.
The water temperatures used are T1/T3 = 25/10 ◦C. The UA is 750 kW/K
and the storage has a total volume VST = 10000 m3. All calculations are made
for hour = 1.
The results in one calculations does not inﬂuence the next
calculation, thus not dynamic behavior.
1. Storage volume VST,c = 0 m3.
It is not possible to draw cold water from the storage to supplement the
FC and VCC. Great potential for charging the storage.
2. Storage volume VST,c = 250 m3.
Cold water can be drawn from the storage, but not enough for storage
to be the only source of cooling in a full hour. Still great potential for
charging the storage.
3. Storage volume VST,c = 5000 m3.
Cold water can be drawn from the storage and the storage can be the
only source of cooling in a full hour. Still great potential for charging the
storage.
==PAGE:83==
7.4. MODEL TEST AND VERIFICATION
69
4. Storage volume VST,c = 9750 m3.
Cold water can be drawn from the storage and the storage can be the
only source of cooling in a full hour. The storage are almost full, which
limits the maximum charging mass ﬂow rate.
5. Storage volume VST,c = 10000 m3.
Cold water can be drawn from the storage and the storage can be the
only source of cooling in a full hour. The storage is full and can not be
charged.
Two scenarios are here explained:
Scenario 2
The heat transfer rate in the system are plotted as a function of the am-
bient temperature.
An additional plot shows the active state in the par-
ticular calculation.
Figure 7.5 shows the results for an almost empty stor-
age, VST,c = 250 m3, for both the heat rates and the operational states.
The demand,
˙Qd is constant at 10 MW. For temperatures below the limit,
Tlim = −6.45 ◦C, the storage are changed using state 1a . The heat rate from
free cooling exceeds the demand, and the storage is charge with the diﬀerence.
When Tamb becomes larger than Tlim the dry coolers can per deﬁnition not
cool the full mass ﬂow rate to the temperature T3. Now the ﬁrst priority is
state
3 , where the 250 m3 in the storage can be used to supplement the dry
coolers. At around Tamb = 4 ◦C is it not possible for the storage to discharge
in magnitude that is required (> 250m3/h). The algorithm proceeds to the
next priority, which is state
6 . The dry coolers runs at full capacity and the
VCC takes the remaining. The need for VCC increases with increasing ambient
temperature. In the range from T3 to T1 the ﬁrst priority is to use the storage,
but as it is not able to provide a full hour the system moves to state
6 . For
Tamb > T1 the system chooses state
7
as expected.
Scenario 4
The case is a bit diﬀerent when the storage is almost full. For the very low
ambient temperatures throttling of dry coolers needed not to exceed the max-
imum cold volume in the tank. This can be seen in Figure 7.6 where the ˙QF C
hits a cap at −17.5 ◦C and goes from state 1a to 1b . Above −17.5 ◦C the free
cooling are able to charge the storage in the same manner an empty storage.
Because the storage is almost full one can use state
3
from Tlim to T3. The
˙QF C curve follows a linear trend up to approximately 7 ◦C where the curve
starts to bend. The gradient is decreasing as the ambient temperature reach
T3 where ˙QF C = 0. The heat rate drawn from the storage is follow the exact
opposite trend. From T3 and up will the storage be the only source of cooling
as state
4
is prioritized above using the VCC.
From these graphs it can be seen that the algorithm made for system 1 acts
according to the prioritized operational control scheme presented in Figure 7.2.
The use of free cooling and storage always acts before the vapor compression
cycle is activated.
The graphs for all ﬁve scenarios can be found in Appendix C.

7.4.2
Hourly operation - inﬂuence of heat transfer capacity
The following section gives some examples of how the heat rates and storage
level change during a full year of operation. The example show the inﬂuence
of increasing heat transfer capacitance, UA, with a constant storage volume of
VST = 750000 m3 for a the water temperatures T1 = 25 ◦C and T3 = 10 ◦C.
Figure 7.7 shows a system with a relative small dry cooler, UA = 1000 kW/K.
From Figure 7.7a shows the heat rate and the ambient temperature. The dry
coolers are only able to charge the storage during few hours during the very
coldest period from hour 1500-4000, where the dry coolers have overcapacity.
The limit temperature is −0.5 ◦C.
It can be seen that the storage are dis-
charged shortly after - a result of the operational state
3 , where the storage
supplement the dry coolers. Due to the very small overcapacity the storage
level is never charged to more than 6-7%. The vapor compression cycle are
used most of the year with the largest load share in the warmest period.

When the UA is doubled to 2000 kW/K the limit temperature increases to
7.5 ◦C. The result of this is more hours for which it is possible to charge the
storage. Figure 7.8a shows that it already from the beginning are possible to
charge the storage. First from around hour 1000 is it possible to store signif-
icant amounts of cold water. The dry coolers produce up to four times the
demand. At time 2600 the storage are fully charged which results in throt-
tled dry cooler capacity. The capacity are adjusted such that the free cooling
matches the demand for a long time during the coldest period. When the am-
bient temperature starts to increase the discharge of the storage starts. From
time 5000-6500 the dry coolers are able to deliver a part of the total mass ﬂow
rate to the data center, the rest is supplied from the storage, state
3 . The
rest of the year the storage is empty, and the cooling demand must be covered
by the VCC.

A closer look at the UA = 4000 kW/K case in the time frame from 800-1600
h are shown in Figure 7.10. This time frame show the operation change from
charging the storage to throttling the dry coolers to match the demand. The
ﬁrst 600 hours the storage are more or less charged all the time interrupted by
a few hours of discharging. The discharging happens due to the high ambient
temperature exceeding the target temperature T3, where the storage covers
the full load. The discharge can be see in Figure 7.10b as a drop in storage
==PAGE:87==
7.4. MODEL TEST AND VERIFICATION
73
level around time 1000h. When the storage level reaches 100% the fans are
throttled.
The time frame 6500-7500 h shows the transition from having cold water in
storage to an empty one, see Figure 7.11.
In this time frame the ambient
temperature exceeds the target temperature T3 in pretty much all hours, which
means that the dry coolers can not act alone. The priority according to the
control scheme is to use the storage for a full hour if possible, otherwise use
a combination of FC and VCC. The storage level starts at 45% - plenty to
use storage as only source of cooling. The discharge are used until time 7100
except for a few hours with charging. At time 7100 the storage level is 0% and
the system starts to operate as state
6 , where the share of VCC increases
with increasing ambient temperature.

CHAPTER 7. SYSTEM 1, SERIES FC AND VCC
7.4.3
Hourly operation - inﬂuence of storage capacity
In this section the inﬂuence of the storage capacity is discussed. The yearly
operation of three diﬀerent storage volumes are investigated for three of the
six temperature sets. The volumes are 187500 m3, 750000 m3 and 1500000 m3.
The UA value of the dry coolers are 2000 kW/K and 8000 kW/K.
Figure 7.12(a) and Figure 7.12(b) are for the temperature set 25/10 ◦C. The
graphs shown the storage level in percent during a full year simulation. Fig-
ure 7.12(a) has UA = 2000 kW/K, which limits the charging of the storage to
ambient temperatures below the limit temperature Tlim = 7.5◦C. It can be
seen that the storage is charged and discharged a little during the ﬁrst 1000
hours. First from hour 1000 do the dry coolers have overcapacity to signiﬁ-
cantly charge - this combined with the fact that the discharge of the storage not
being needed. The smaller the storage volume is the faster the 100% storage
level is reached. The 1500000 m3 storage takes the whole cold season to fully
charge, but can then be discharged throughout the most of the summer season.
Increasing the UA to 8000 kW/K, increases the limit temperature to 9.9◦C. A
consequence is that the charging can start at higher ambient temperature and
at with a larger mass ﬂow rate due to the increased overcapacity. The charging
start from the beginning of the simulations and all three volumes reach 100%
storage level within the ﬁrst 1200 hours. During the cold season the storage
level remains 100%. Around 6000 the storage starts to discharge just as in
Figure 7.12(a), but are able to recharge due to the higher Tlim. The actual
discharge due to too high ambient temperature starts 1000 hours later. The
1500000 m3 option are subject to the forced discharge at the end of the year
already from hour 6500, whereas the smaller volumes ﬁrst are force to discharge
at the very end.
The temperature set in Figure 7.12(c) and Figure 7.12(d) are 35/20 ◦C. The
limit temperature for the low UA is 17.5◦C which allows for storage charging
91% of the year. For the 8000 kW/K the limit temperature is 19.9◦C which al-
lows for charging 95% of the year. For both UA the charging starts at the very
beginning of the simulation and the storage level increases rapidly to 100%.
The hours where the storage are used to deliver cooling are in the summer
period, where the ambient temperatures are above 20 ◦C. All the storage are
subject to the forced end-of-year discharge.
The ﬁnal temperature set increases the target temperature to 30◦C.
Fig-
ure 7.12(e) and Figure 7.12(f) are very similar to those for 35/20 ◦C, how-
ever the charging is even faster. The limit temperatures for 2000 kW/K and
8000 kW/K are 27.5◦C and 29.9◦C, respectively. The large storage volumes are
not really needed for T3 = 30◦C as only 14 and 4 hours are above the limit
temperature.
As expected the larger the storage volume, the longer charge time and the more
discharge time in the end.

7.5
Results
7.5.1
Annual power consumption
The power consumption for dry coolers and vapor compression cycle for each
the 8760 hours in a year has been summed to an annual power consumption
of the system. 486 simulations have been made with system 1 for diﬀerent
temperature sets, storage volumes and dry cooler heat transfer capacitance.
All the annual system power consumption are presented in Figure 7.13. The
ﬁgure consists of six tables, one for each temperature set.
The tables are
matrices with increasing UA to the right and increasing installed storage when
going downwards. The colors in the heat map are red to green, where green
represent the lowest power consumption and red the highest. The tables use
the same color reference for the heat map (Green 10 to Red 10800). This makes
it easy to compare the temperature sets. The downside is that it is harder to
locate local minimums.
In the following the reference to cells in the tables are of the form:
(T1/T3, VST , UA)
An example could be (15/10,187500,2000) meaning temperature set 15/10 ◦C
with 187500 m3 storage and 2000 kW/K dry cooler.
The coordinate (T1/T3,0,0) is equal to system 0, where the VCC are the only
component. For a conﬁguration with 0 kW/K dry cooler one would not observe
changes in the power consumption with increasing the storage volume. This
is due to the assumption that VCC can not charge the storage - implementing
the storage has no impact. From the ﬁgure it is clear that the VCC acting a
alone as system 0 yields the highest power consumption (red values).
By introducing dry coolers with a UA = 1000 kW/K great energy savings
are obtained for all temperature sets, 49%-95%, largest for high target tem-
peratures. The savings increase further by adding more dry cooler capacity.
For the temperature set 15/10 ◦C trend goes towards a minimum value at
(15/10,1500000,8000). Having the low target temperature T3 = 10◦C requires
a large storage to be able to utilize it as long as possible when the ambient
temperature is above. The ∆T = 5K cause the required mass ﬂow rate to
the data center, ˙mw,d to be high, thus the stored water can last for a shorter
period. The large UA is a result of maximizing the charging of storage. The
same observations can be seen for the temperature set 25/20 ◦C. The optimum
is also maximum storage volume, but smaller UA (25/10,1500000,6000).
When increasing the target temperature T3 one can see a signiﬁcant drop in
power consumption. The explanation for this is that the VCC operates at a
higher evaporation temperature which gives a higher COP. Furthermore the
amount of hours with potential for 100% free cooling increases. For T3 = 20◦C
and T3 = 30◦C it is 95% and 99.9% of the year, respectively.
The mimimum power consumption for 25/20 ◦C and 35/20 ◦C are at the same
conﬁguration: 187500 m3 storage and 8000 kW/K. The dry coolers should be
maxed out allowing for high overcapacity for charging and low average fan ca-
pacity, thus minimal power consumption. Some storage are needed, but as the

7.5.2
Economic valuation
A general consideration: To get a high NPV the investments and power con-
sumption should be minimized. As seen in Figure 7.13 the power consumption
decreases with larger components, but that contradicts to the desired small
investment. The investment will drag the NPV-optimum toward 0 m3 stor-
age and 0 kW/K dry cooler, and the power consumption towards the power
optimum. The NPV-optimum would be located where the energy savings are
suﬃcient to cover the additional investment.
The present values in M’DKK of system 1 can be seen in Figure 7.14. The col-
ors in the heat map are red to green, where green represent the highest present
value and red the lower. The more green the better. The tables use the same
color reference for the heat map (Green -57 to Red -680, green being the best
option).
Using equation (4.6) results in all present values getting negative. The reason
for this is the lack of revenues when operating the system. However, as for all
NPV analysis the project acceptance criteria is the project with the highest
present value. In this case one should go for the least negative.
A brief look at the tables shows that the cooling project is getting cheaper by
raising the target temperature. For T3 = 10◦C the beneﬁt of implement storage
and dry coolers are almost non existing, visualized by the red/dark orange color.
The optimum for 15/10 ◦C can be found in the coordinate (15/10,1000,0). A
==PAGE:93==
7.5. RESULTS
79
solution with just a little free cooling capacity but no storage.
The power
savings are simply not enough for other conﬁgurations and the present value
decreases signiﬁcant. The implementation of the 1000 kW/K dry cooler gives
a large energy saving for a low cost.
The temperature set 25/10 ◦C acts very similar with a little higher present
value due to the lower power consumption. An interesting thing though is the
fact that the present value starts to increase again for the large values of stor-
age and heat transfer capacitance. The present value increases such that the
optimum can be found in (25/10,5000,1500000) having the value -398 M’DKK.
This can be explained with Figure 7.15, which shows the maximum heat rate
from the vapor compression cycle. It can be interpreted as the installed capac-
ity. The combinations of large storage and many dry coolers can decrease the
necessary installed VCC capacity. Less VCC mean a lower capital investment,
the most inﬂuential term in the NPV calculation. Even though the economical
optimum are located with large components one might consider the practicality
aspect and uncertainty of the cost correlation. This temperature set do also
have a low present value the same place as 15/10 ◦C, namely (25/10,1000,0).
The diﬀerence in present value is 10 M’DKK.
As the target temperature increases the optimums are more distinct. It is now
possible to make conﬁgurations that completely eliminate the need for VCCs.
The present value drops signiﬁcantly when the VCC are omitted. For 25/20 ◦C
the optimum is (25/20,4000,1875000), a combination that provide suﬃcient
energy savings for implementing a ’small’ storage. The present value is -118
M’DKK. The same is true for 35/20 ◦C, but due to the larger ∆Tw less heat
transfer capacitance will do. The present value of (35/20,2000,187500) is -79
M’DKK. The optima for 35/30 ◦C and 45/30 ◦C are (35/30,2000,187500) and
(45/30,1000,187500).
The drops in NPV due to no vapor compression cycle are signiﬁcant for conﬁg-
urations with smaller components For increasing storage volume and UA the
power savings can not cover the extra investment resulting in NPV to increase
- even though the no VCC are installed.
The optimum values for the present value are collected in Table 7.2.

CHAPTER 7. SYSTEM 1, SERIES FC AND VCC
7.5.3
Optimal conﬁgurations
This section summarizes both the power consumption optimum solutions and
the present value optimum solutions. Figure 7.16 shows the the annual power
consumption for both the power and present value optima.
Power optimum case: The annual power consumption decreases rapidly towards
zero when increasing the target temperature and the temperature diﬀerence of
the water. The associated present values does increase, but the trend is not as
clear.
Present optimum case: Here the focus is to maximize the present value. The
results show that increasing temperature and temperature diﬀerence means
increasing present value. The power consumption does also decrease.
For all temperature set the NPV optimized solutions yield higher present value
than the power optimized, regardless of the higher power consumption. One
can conclude that the consumption are of less importance when it comes to
economy compared to the investment.

Chapter 8
System 2, Series VCC and FC
8.1
System description
System 2 is a series setup where the water returning from the data center ﬁrst
enters the vapor compression cycle and then dry coolers. The thermal storage
is parallel to the ’cooling string’. The water returns from the data center with
temperature T1. After the VCC the water has temperature T2. If it is not
possible to use free cooling then T2 = T3. The VCC brings the temperature
from T1 to T2. The temperature T2 depends on the ambient temperature and
the UA of the dry cooler.
The storage can be charged if the dry coolers have overcapacity, which requires
that the ambient temperature T11 < Tlim. In that case the dry coolers are
able to bring the temperature from T1 to T3 with a higher mass ﬂow rate
than needed for the data center, ˙mw > ˙mw,d. The additional mass ﬂow rate is
induced by the storage loop, ˙mw = ˙mST + ˙mw,d. The water circulates clockwise
in the storage loop, while the water ﬂow ˙mw,d is maintained.
The storage can be discharge by reversing the ﬂow to the storage. The mass
balance for the joint after returning from the data center with T1 is ˙mw,d =
˙mST + ˙mw.
˙mw,d
T1
˙mw
T2

==PAGE:98==

CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM 2, SERIES VCC AND FC
8.2
System speciﬁc assumptions
System 2 has the same assumptions as system 1. The ﬂow direction of the
’cooling string’ can only be from left to right.
T1 →T2 →T3
The ’cooling string’ in the cycle layout in Figure 7.1 is drawn such that the
water ﬂow is through both the dry cooler and the vapor compression cycle at all
times. In principle one would observe a heat ingress for ambient temperature
above T1. In practice it should be possible to by-pass the dry cooler, otherwise
the temperature T2 could end up being higher than T1. In the simulations full
by-pass is assumed.
8.3
Operational states and control
The operational control scheme is presented in Figure 8.2. The numbers refer
to the state numbers in Table 5.2.
For ambient temperatures below the limit temperature system 2 follows what is
described in section 5.1.3, identical to system 1. The ﬁrst priority is to charge
the storage using state 1a . If storage is full the fans are throttled to match
maximum charge mass ﬂow rate with state 1b .
What happens for Tamb > T1 are furthermore similar to the system 1. The
ﬁrst priority is to use cold water from the storage if available, state
4 . If the
water level in the storage does not meet a full hour demand, the storage will
be supplemented by the VCC, state
7 . The very last priority, state
8
is
used if the storage is empty.
For ambient temperatures in the interval [Tlim ; T3[ the priorities are also sim-
ilar to the other systems. It is always prioritized to use free cooling as much as
possible and here with supplement from the storage, state
3 . Even though
the second priority also here are state
6
the results be diﬀerent due to the
component order.
The largest diﬀerence from this system layout to the system 1 is what hap-
pens in the interval [T3 ; T1[.
Having the dry cooler as the last component
means that it should be able to deliver water at T3. This is not possible when
Tamb > T3. The operational states in this temperature interval are therefore
identical to what happens above Tamb > T1.
Illustrations of the operational states can be seen in Figure F.2 and F.3 in
appendix. The active components are colored in red. The water ﬂow direction
are illustrated with arrows. State
5
is also shown but in active components
are gray (’excluded gray’). The arrow inside the storage shows whether it is
charged or discharged. An arrow upwards means charging - the cold volume
increases.

8.4
Model test and veriﬁcation
8.4.1
Static test
The static test of the operational control scheme for system 2 are made ac-
cording to the description in subsection 7.4.1. The EES code can be found in
Appendix I. One scenario are shown and described here, the graphs for the
other static test can be found in Appendix D.
Scenario 2
The scenario where the storage level are close to zero (250 m3). A full hour of
discharging the storage acting as the only source of cooling requires 574 m3.
Figure 8.3 shows the heat rates and active operational state for ambient tem-
perature between −20◦C and 30◦C.
For ambient temperatures below the limit temperature Tlim the dry coolers
charge the storage. The heat rate add to the storage equals the overcapacity
of the dry coolers. The overcapacity decreases as the ambient temperature
approaches Tlim.
The maximum heat rate of the dry coolers follow the same downwards going
trend when the temperature exceeds the limit. The free cooling heat rate are
supplemented by a heat rate from the storage, state
3 , to match the demand.
At some point the storage cannot provide the enough and the operational con-
trol scheme shifts to the next priority - state
6 combining vapor compression
cycle and dry cooler. It can can be seen that the ˙QV CC is the most dominant
taking the large load share. The discontinuity in the free cooling heat rate
˙QF C is due to the iteration of temperature T2.
When Tamb > T3 free cooling cannot be used anymore. The control scheme
prioritize state
4 , which is full load share from the storage. The storage ca-
pacity in this case is not suﬃcient, why it is supplemented with VCC - state
7
The model acts according to the operational control scheme in Figure 8.2.

8.4.2
Hourly operation - inﬂuence of heat transfer capacity
The following section gives the same examples as for system 1 of how the heat
rates and storage level change during a full year of operation. The example
show the inﬂuence of increasing heat transfer capacitance, UA, with a constant
storage volume of VST = 750000 m3 for a the water temperatures T1 = 25 ◦C
and T3 = 10 ◦C. The storage level proﬁles are identical to system 1. The rea-
son is that charging happens at the same hours and in the same speed. The
discharging is also in he same hours as the operational schemes are identical at
this point (ﬁrst priorities).
Figure 8.4 shows a year of operation with UA = 1000 kW/K. The heat rates
are in Figure 8.4a and the storage level in Figure 8.4b. As for system 1 1000
kW/K are not enough to charge the storage for long term storing.
In the
warmest hours the vapor compression cycle acts alone or in combination with
the dry cooler. It is clear that the VCC are most dominant and take a large
share of the load. An example of that can be seen from hour 5000-8760. For all
temperatures above T3 = 10◦C are the VCC the only source of cooling. This
is very diﬀerent to system 1, where free cooling is possible to some degree up
to Tamb = T1.
The UA is increased to 2000 kW/K and 4000 kW/K in the Figure F.4 and Fig-
ure F.5 in Appendix F. The same trends as for system 1 can be seen. Increasing
UA will result in faster charging as a consequence of larger overcapacity. The

8.4.3
Hourly operation - inﬂuence of storage capacity
The yearly operation of three diﬀerent storage volumes are investigated for
three of the six temperature sets. The volumes are 187500 m3, 750000 m3 and
1500000 m3. The UA value of the dry coolers are 2000 kW/K and 8000 kW/K
as before.
The results are to be found in Figure F.1 in Appendix F. They are identical
to the ones found for system 1, Figure 7.12, with the same argumentation.
The operational control schemes dictate charging at the same times due to the
deﬁnition of Tlim. The discharge does also happen in the same hours.
8.5
Results
8.5.1
Annual power consumption
The power consumption for dry coolers and vapor compression cycle for each
the 8760 hours in a year has been summed to an annual power consumption of
the system. 486 simulations have been made with system 2 for diﬀerent tem-
perature sets, storage volumes and dry cooler heat transfer capacitance. All
the annual system power consumption are presented in Figure 8.7. The ﬁgure
consists of six tables, one for each temperature set. The tables are matrices
with increasing UA to the right and increasing installed storage when going
downwards. The colors in the heat map are red to green, where green represent
the lowest power consumption and red the highest. The tables use the same
color reference for the heat map (Green 10 to Red 10800).
The trends in power consumption for the six temperature sets are the same as
system 1 described in subsection 7.5.1. The power optimum conﬁgurations are
also here located with large dry cooler heat transfer capacitance. Low target
temperatures T3 requires large storage capacity. The trend is that the storage
==PAGE:103==
8.5. RESULTS
89
capacity decreases with increasing target temperature, as the amount of hours
above Tamb > T3 decreases which can be seen in the ambient temperature dis-
tribution chart. The power optimum conﬁgurations are presented in Table 8.1.
The main parts of the tables are with the same or nearly same power usage as
system 1, however a few places are the diﬀerence relatively large. For conﬁg-
urations with UA = 1000 kW/K are the diﬀerence signiﬁcant. For 25/10 ◦C is
the diﬀerence ≈2200 MWh/yr. This can be described with the observations
in Figure 7.7a where the VCC contributes massively in the load share.

8.5.2
Economic valuation
The similarity of the power consumption for system 1 and 2 results in similar
present value tables as well, see Figure 8.8. The conﬁgurations with no stor-
age yield lower present values due to more installed VCC capacity compared
to system 1. System 2 has always need 10 MW VCC capacity except for a
few conﬁgurations, see Figure F.6. This gives higher investment costs, thus
smaller NPV. The reason can be found in the series connection. If the ambient
temperature is above T3 it is not possible to use the dry coolers at all why
the vapor compression cycle must cope with the full cooling demand (10 MW).
System 1 has the advantage that the can lower the temperature into the VCC
for temperatures up to T1.
When storage is added there is really no diﬀerence in NPV. The temperature
set 25/10 ◦C that in system 1 could lead to an optimum with 1500000 m3
storage are not present for system 2 as the need for 10MW VCC are in all
conﬁgurations.
The economic optimum conﬁguration are summarized in Table 8.2.

8.5.3
Optimal conﬁgurations
This section summarizes both the power consumption optimum solutions and
the present value optimum solutions. Figure 8.9 shows the the annual power
consumption for both the power and present value optima.
Power optimum case: The annual power consumption decreases rapidly towards
zero when increasing the target temperature and the temperature diﬀerence of
the water. The associated present values does increase, but the trend is not as
clear.
Present optimum case: Here the focus is to maximize the present value. The
results show that increasing temperature and temperature diﬀerence means
increasing present value. The power consumption does in general decrease, but
at 25/10◦C the power consumption peaks.
For all temperature set do the NPV optimized solutions yield higher present
value than the power optimized, regardless of the higher power consumption.
One can conclude that the consumption are of less importance when it comes
to economy.

Chapter 9
System 3, Parallel VCC and FC
9.1
System description
The third and ﬁnal system layout are a parallel setup of the three main compo-
nents shown in Figure 9.1. Again water returns with the temperature T1 and
mass ﬂow rate ˙mw,d. The pipe layout allows for the mass ﬂow to split and go
to either of the three components directly in an arbitrary split ratio. The mass
ﬂow rate in the storage branch can be in either directions if it is charged or
discharged. The ﬂow direction in the dry cooler and vapor compression cycle
can only be downwards.
The dry coolers cool the water from T1 to T3,F C. The VCC cools from T1 to
T3,V CC. The mixing point at the outlets should end up with a temperature
equal to T3 such that water meets the demands from the data center. The stor-
age can be charged when the dry coolers have overcapacity due to low ambient
temperatures. In that case the mass ﬂow rate through the dry coolers increase
while T3,F C = T3. The additional mass ﬂow are led to the storage increasing
the cold volume. The shrinking warm volume induce the additional mass ﬂow
rate the the dry coolers. The water circulates clockwise between storage and
dry coolers.

CHAPTER 9. SYSTEM 3, PARALLEL VCC AND FC
9.2
System speciﬁc assumptions
The mass ﬂow rate in the storage branch can be in either directions if it is
charged or discharged. The ﬂow direction in the dry cooler and vapor com-
pression cycle can only be downwards.
9.3
Operational states and control
The given system layout arises the quest whether it is better to control both
the FC and VCC water outlet temperature at the desired temperature T3 or
let the VCC cool the water to a lower temperature, T3,V CC < T3 and mix with
a potential higher temperature from FC T3,F C. The last option increases the
annual utility of the FC as it can be used for temperatures above T3.
The interesting part is for ambient temperatures in the range Tlim < Tamb < T1
as described in subsection 5.1.3.
A simple investigation of the options are
conducted. They are referred to as EQL and VAR.
EQL
represent the case where the system operates such that T3,F C = T3 and
T3,V CC = T3. The mixing point only receives water at the desired temperature.
This means that the FC is not able to work for ambient temperatures above
T3. For cases Tamb ≥T3 the VCC deals with the total demand, ˙Qd. The VCC
evaporation temperature is constant and given by T3,V CC as TE = T3,V CC−5K.
Figure 9.2 shows the operational states. From Tlim →T3 the system share the
load between FC and VCC. The FC is running at full capacity and maximizes
the heat transfer in this component. The VCC acts like a slave and contributes
with the remain demand.
Tamb
Tlim
T3
T1
6
8
Figure 9.2: Control scheme for EQL model, numbers refer to Table 5.2.
VAR
represent the case where the system mixes unequal temperatures from
the FC and VCC. The VCC provide water temperature based on a ﬁxed evap-
oration temperature, T3,V CC = TE + 5K. Energy and mass balances for the
mixing point ensures that the water temperature T3 to the data center. The
share of mass ﬂow rates are therefore determined according to what the FC is
capable of at a given ambient temperature due to the UA. For this case the
the operational state is always a combination of FC and VCC, Figure 9.3.
The total system COP are calculated for each case for ambient temperatures
between Tlim and T1 for all six temperature sets. The results can be found
in Appendix L. An example is Figure 9.4. It can be observed that for some
ambient temperature the highest COP, thus lowest power consumption, will

be from EQL and others VAR. The EQL option is the worst for temperatures up to
approximately 15◦C, point B , hereafter it is the best. The curve has an edge in
point A at 10◦C - this is where the control scheme shift to full VCC operation.
The edge in point B are due to the max deﬁnition of condensing temperature in
the VCC. One should consider what the power consumption is on annual basis

for both options to be able to choose the most eﬃcient. One could combine
these curves with the ambient temperature distribution curve to quantify the
power consumption for the temperatures within the interval Tlim to T1. This
example uses a rather small UA, which gives a low limit temperature. If the
UA is increased (increasing Tlim), the temperature interval, where VAR is the
best, is decrease. If a storage is implemented the number of hours where a
diﬀerence could be observed would be limited even more. For these reasons the
option with T3,F C = T3 and T3,V CC = T3 are chosen.
The operational control scheme for system 3 can be seen in Figure 9.5.

9.4
Model test and veriﬁcation
9.4.1
Static test
The static test is with the same approach as described in subsection 7.4.1. The
most interesting part of the test is for an almost empty storage, see Figure 9.6.
The storage is charged up to the point where the ambient temperature exceeds
Tlim. Hereafter the dry coolers are only able to cool a share of the mass ﬂow
rate to the target temperature ( ˙mw,F C < ˙mw,d). The storage supplement the
remaining.
At some point is the storage not able to deliver enough cold water and the
operational scheme jumps to the next priority, state
6 . What is interesting
is that the free cooling heat rate continues to deliver the maximum mass ﬂow
rate as possible.
Instead of the storage supplementing it is now the vapor
compression cycle. This happens all the way to T3. At temperatures above T3
free cooling is not possible and the operation continues in state
7 .
The other graphs can be found in Appendix E.
9.4.2
Hourly operation - inﬂuence of heat transfer capacity
The same examples for hourly operation as system 1 and 2 are made for system
3. Figure 9.7 shows the initial example with UA = 1000 kW/K. The graph
is very similar to the one for system 2. The operational control schemes are
identical, but small changes can be seen as state
6
behaves diﬀerently as an
consequence of the cycle layout. From time 6000-8000 can the diﬀerence be
seen. System 3 are able to use a larger share free cooling.
More hourly operations can be seen in Appendix G.
9.4.3
Hourly operation - inﬂuence of storage capacity
The results are to be found in Figure G.1 in Appendix G. They are identical
to the ones found for system 1 and 2, Figure 7.12 and Figure F.1, with the
same argumentation.

9.5.2
Economic valuation
In general the present value evaluation of the system 3 conﬁguration are iden-
tical with system 1 and 2 with respect to trends. The maximum present values

is above 1,125,000 m3. The installed capacity can be seen in Appendix G.

9.5.3
Optimal conﬁgurations
The power consumption optimum solutions and the present value optimum so-
lutions are presented in Figure 8.9. The power consumption for the two cases of
target temperature T3 = 10◦C are higher than what are seen for system 1 and 2
for present value optimum. The inﬂuence increased consumption does not have
great inﬂuence on the present value. As discussed earlier is the capital invest-
ment the most dominant, and a relative small change in power consumption is
insigniﬁcant for the result.

Chapter 10
System comparison
This section summarizes the results from the previous chapters. All conﬁg-
urations of the dry cooler heat transfer capacitance and storage volume are
collected in Table 10.1. As wrote in subsection 7.5.2 a high NPV can be ob-
tained if the investments and power consumption are minimized. The power
consumption decreases with larger components, but that contradicts the de-
sired for small investment. The investment will drag the NPV-optimum toward
0 m3 storage and 0 kW/K dry cooler, and the power consumption towards the
power optimum. The NPV-optimum is where the energy savings are suﬃcient
to cover the additional investment.
The temperature set 15/10◦C yields the same conﬁgurations for the power op-
timum: UA = 8000kW/K and VST = 1, 500, 000m3. The power consumption
for system 1 is 4666 MWh/yr, which is 600 MWh/yr higher than the two other
systems. One of these solutions are chosen, the energy savings compared to the
pure VCC conﬁguration is in magnitude of 6000 MWh/yr, thus 60% savings.
The present values for these conﬁgurations are approximately -680 M’DKK.
Looking at the present value optimums the present value increases to -411 for
system 1 and 2, while system 3 gives -441. The diﬀerence comes from the in-
vestment of a larger VCC installed in system 3.
Similar trends can be seen for 25/10◦C. The larger ∆Tw allows for a smaller
UA, which is why it can be decreased to 6000kW/K. A massive storage vol-
ume is still desired as it is possible to discharge for a larger period the summer,
where the average temperature is above 10◦C. The power consumption drop
to a forth of the one from 15/10◦C. The present values increases, especially for
system 1, as a smaller VCC can be installed. The series connection dry cool-
ers ﬁrst allows utilization for ambient temperature up to 25◦C. The present
value optimums are interesting. The system 1 has an odd optimum compared
to the other models. System 1 requires a VST = 1, 500, 000m3, where as the
others have zero. Even though the investment are signiﬁcant the power saving
is enough to make the solution the best of the three. If the second option for
system 1 is chosen, the present value go from -398 M’DKK to -408 M’DKK. It
is 10 M’DKK that one might consider investing to avoid the enormous storage.
When the target temperature increases further power savings can be obtained.
The potential for free cooling increases for all the system layouts. The tem-
perature sets 25/20◦C and 35/20◦C have the same optimum conﬁgurations for
103
==PAGE:118==
104

10.1. ACCUMULATED POWER CONSUMPTION
105
all the system layouts.
UA = 8000kW/K and the smallest storage VST =
187, 000m3. The power consumption is smaller for large ∆Tw, but even though
the power consumption is half, the beneﬁt in present value are only 3 M’DKK.
At these temperature sets it is possible to choose conﬁgurations that avoid
installation of VCC, thus increase the present value signiﬁcant. The economic
optimization yield −118 M’DKK and −79 M’DKK.
If the target temperature is increased even further, the hours for which free
cooling is impossible are very limited. The present value optimized solutions
are the same for all three systems as the operational control scheme never enters
the priorities, where the diﬀerence between the models are. If just the smallest
storage are implemented there is no need for a VCC. The present value for
45/30◦C is -57 M’DKK.
The general trends regarding power optimized solutions is that increasing tar-
get temperature yield decreasing annual power consumption. The very low 10
MWh/yr for 45/30◦C are a consequence of the largest dry coolers installed
running a at very low fan speed. The equation for the fan power consumption
explain that it is near zero, what would be close to passive cooling. A large
temperature diﬀerence of the water yield lower power consumption. For power
optimizing one should go for the largest UA possible. The storage volume de-
pends on the target temperature. Low target temperature need large storage
volume. There is no trend for the associated present value.
With respect to the present value optimized solutions one could say: A small to
medium UA and a small storage volume yields the best economy. Is it possible
to choose a conﬁgurations that do not need VCC it would be beneﬁcial. The
control scheme and system layouts do not inﬂuence the present value optima.
If the data center can allow high water temperatures very signiﬁcant savings
can be obtained, and the data center can gain a green and environmental pro-
ﬁle.
10.1
Accumulated power consumption
The present value optimum cases for the temperature set 25/10◦C are plotted
in Figure 10.1. This one is chosen as it is the only temperature set, where a
diﬀerence in present value can be observed, see Table 10.1. The accumulated
power consumption is plotted vs the time for a full year. It can be seen that
system 0, the option only with VCC, accumulate to a linear curve up to 10.8
GWh.
The true optimum solution for system 1 includes a massive storage
volume and a rather large UA starts with a somewhat step gradient meaning
that the hourly power consumption is high, the curve ﬂattens at time 1500 and
from here only have a small consumption the rest of the year.
The optimum conﬁgurations for system 2 and 3 are also plotted. These con-
ﬁguration does not include a storage volume. It can be seen that the power
consumption in the beginning of the year is just as large as for system 0. The
reason being a large share of the load on VCCs. When the ambient tempera-
ture drops during the winter the potential for free cooling increases and causes
power consumption to slow down. When the ambient temperature starts to
==PAGE:120==

rise again do the VCC share increase again. It can be seen that system 2 has
the highest power consumption during the full year compared to system 1 and

Figure 10.1: Accumulated power consumption for 25/10◦C
Figure Figure 10.2 shows the accumulated power consumption and storage lev-
els for a full year simulation for one temperature set. The power consumption
shows a high power consumption during the winter period until the storage is
full. After this point, only a small amount of power is required to meet the
cooling demand by the dry coolers. At around 5000 hours, the start of sum-
mer, the power consumption increases as the dry coolers has to increase the
fan speed in order the cover the demand. Due to the forced-discharge rule, the
storage will start discharging and cover most of the demand in the remaining
period, resulting in only a slight increase in accumulated power consumption.

==PAGE:124==
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 11
Discussion
11.1
Assumptions
While conducting the project, a number of assumptions and simpliﬁcations
was necessary in order simulate the data center cooling system. Several key
parameters are worth considering and discussing, as their impact on the overall
system could be signiﬁcant.
The storage is one of the key components in the system layouts why the assump-
tion and modeling of this plays a role. Several and rather large assumptions
are made to ease the calculations, but they come with a cost. The storage
used in the models are what could be interpreted as a perfect storage with no
losses, temperature mixing or other limitations. When the storage is charge is
it able to store the cold water for an inﬁnite period of time without capacity
changes. In the real world this is not the case. Heat transfer between the
water in the storage and the ambient will be present. This means that when
the ambient temperature is above the water temperature heat will ingress and
the temperature in the storage increase. The water can also be cooled by the
ambient. Normally storage both tank and pits are well insulated to avoid the
concerns. The model is made with two volumes that does not interact. In re-
ality water storage like these would have mixing as a consequence of Buoyancy
ﬂows and/or in and out ﬂows. The hot and cold zones would over time mix to
a uniform temperature. A consequence of the mixing would be that it might
be impossible to discharge the water directly to the data center. The capacity
of the storage is likely smaller than expected. The model could be improved by
better modeling of the stratiﬁcation by dividing the total storage volume into
a number of smaller volumes that interact with each other and the ambient.
The vapor compression cycle is modeled using the very simple Carnot COP
method. This method is used to predict the power consumption of the VCC
on the basis of the evaporation and condensation temperatures - temperatures
that are assumed with ﬁxed temperature diﬀerences to the ambient and cooling
water. The method does not take the operational limitation that a ’real’ cycle
would have into account. If the VCC was modeled with a higher level of details
deﬁning the physical components sizes, then limitations on partial load would
be seen. The partial load performance is demanding on the ambient and water
temperatures, UA of the evaporator and condenser, displacement volume in
111
==PAGE:126==
112
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION
compressor and so on. Such a system would likely not be able to operate from
0-100% of dimensional load like the assumption states. The minimum partial
load could be 30% as an example. A result of that would be that those hours
where the VCC should add what corresponds to 5% load overcapacity would
occur. The extra cooling could either be added to the storage or the water to
the data center decrease to a temperature below T3.
A better VCC model would likely give a higher power consumption due to
component eﬃciency. The COPC can be a bit optimistic even though a simple
implementation of limitations are made with the max-function used to deter-
mine the condensation temperature.
The charging of the storage is not limited. The maximum mass ﬂow rate for
charging are fully dictated by the dry coolers. As long as they are able to
deliver the water at temperature T3 they will. An example could be that the
dry coolers are able to transfer 80 MW when operating at full capacity. This
results in a mass ﬂow rate through the heat exchangers 8 times larger than the
dimensional case. It could be a problem from a practical point of view. The
heat exchanger geometry and water velocity inﬂuences the pressure losses that
could be high. If a upper limit was implemented in the model, the time it takes
to fully charge the storage would increase. Maybe it would not be possible to
charge the biggest volumes due to the charge time. The way the dry coolers are
modeled allow them to run at full fan speed, thus high power consumption, for
only very small water ﬂows or small ∆Tw. It could be argued that a minimum
temperature decrease of the water should be implemented.
The assumption of no pressure losses in the system results in a lower power con-
sumption than what would actually be observed. It is signiﬁcant water ﬂows
both for the data center, but deﬁnitely also for the circulating water when
charging the storage. The power consumption for pumps might be relevant to
the total power consumption.
11.2
Control scheme
The operational control scheme presented in this project does always prioritize
free cooling over use of storage and vapor compression cycle. The operational
control schemes are very similar, which also explains why the both the power
optimum and NPV optimums are of same magnitude for the three models.
The actions and priorities for ambient temperatures below Tlim and above T1
are the same for all three systems. Furthermore if the conﬁguration includes
some storage volume, the utilization will be the ﬁrst priority for in the interval
[Tlim; T1[. This leads to very few hours where there will be an actual diﬀerence
between the models performance.
One could consider if a more clever control scheme could be made. As it is
now, the storage are charged during the cold season. It is typically fully charged
after a relative short period of time, hereafter the fans are throttled and the
cold water stored for later use - either if the ambient temperature is high or
if the storage is forced to discharge at the end of the year. Maybe it could be
beneﬁcial to use the cold water in the storage before, even in the cold season.
==PAGE:127==
11.3. OTHER CONSIDERATIONS
113
It could be for VST,c,τ−1 is larger than x% of VST,max.
One of the states that actually is possible but still omitted is state
5 , where all
three components are active at the same time. It was omitted as it requires an
optimization algorithm deciding the load share of the three components. The
optimization algorithm could incorporate hourly power prices using the day-a-
head marked and more advanced predictions and forecast on weather data to
ﬁnd the most beneﬁcial load share. It is an optimization of power prices, the
value of the cold water in the storage, what the individual operational costs are
for VCC and FC. Finally it is a question of the availability of the components.
As it is now the VCC are not allowed to charge the storage, but it might be
feasible to let it if the power prices are low. State
5
is out of the scope of
this project.
11.3
Other considerations
It is for sure that the capital investment are of highest importance with respect
to the NPV optimum. The energy savings obtained by implementing a mas-
sive storage and lots of dry coolers are simply not large enough to cover the
additional investment. The savings are too small. The sensitivity analysis of
the economical parameters in section 4.5 showed that parameters relating to
the operational and maintenance costs had small impact on the present value.
In the results for all three models can be seen that choosing the right conﬁgu-
ration of dry cooler and storage volume can eliminate the use for VCC, which
results in a signiﬁcantly lower investment cost.
It is expensive to have a sharp limit on the temperature T3. Not allowing the
temperature to ﬂuctuate within a certain interval (e.g. ±1K) requires very
precise measuring equipment and highly and precisely adjustable components
in the VCC and FC. It can also add extra cost to the investment. For example
with T3 = 30◦C only 6 hours a year have ambient temperatures above T3. The
dry cooler could easily handle all hours if a small change in target temperature
were allowed. The maximum ambient temperature in the DRY data is 32.1◦C.
If this temperature was allowed by to the data center one would save the costs
of a storage or VCC.
The results of the simulations indicates trends towards optima for power con-
sumptioin and present value. The result tables are a mesh of 9 × 9, which is a
rather coarse mesh. The real optima could easily be some where between the
calculated combinations. Example for T3 = 20◦C, where the thermodynamic
optimum is for storage volume of 187500m3. It might be too big, however still
better than zero.
Some of the present value solutions does not need a vapor compression cycle,
and the cooling system for the data center can be made with dry coolers and
storage alone. However questions about redundancy, security of supply and
backup might arise. Data centers rely on high online time, why the cooling
system must work at all times. The solution implemented should consider this
aspect.
==PAGE:128==
114
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION
11.4
Future work
To improve and further generalize the results and trends discussed in this
project, one could consider expanding the system with regard to:
• Consider other climates than the Scandinavian/Northern Europe. It is
likely that the trends and conclusions for a system operation in a signiﬁ-
cantly warmer climate would be quite diﬀerent compared to Copenhagen.
Obviously reaching the lower temperature sets with free cooling would
be harder and more expensive.
• The inclusion of dynamic power prices. This is expected to have a impact
on the overall conclusions. This could be further expanded by integrating
the economic analysis with the thermodynamic model, thus taking into
account the possibility of charging the storage with cheap sustainable
power.
• Implementation of state 5 control scheme. Develop the decision-algorithm
using day-a-head power marked prices to plan operation of the cooling
system.
• Implement and compare diﬀerent kinds of thermal storage types. Both
sensible and latent, while further considering the stratiﬁcation impact on
the the storage performance.
• A more detailed model of individual components, in particular the va-
por compression cycle and storage.
This would yield a more realistic
indication of the cooling system performance.
• The possibility of utilizing heat recovery in conjunction with a heat pump
to produce and sell low temperature district heating for the municipality
in which the data center operates. With the next generation of district
heating, it is possible to utilize even relatively low temperature surplus
heat, both for space heating and warm water production.
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 12
Conclusion
Several diﬀerent system layouts for data center cooling has been investigated
with regard to annual power consumption and present values.
The system
consists of three main components, a dry cooler, a vapor compression cycle
and a sensible thermal energy storage, using water as heat transfer ﬂuid and
storage medium. Quasi-static simulations on hourly basis has been conducted
using EES to evaluate a full year operation performance. Six temperatures sets
has been investigated for increasing heat transfer capacitance UA and storage
volume, resulting in almost 1500 full year simulations.
The results shows general trends in the power consumption. Power optimal
solutions require large free cooling capacity for all temperature sets. The stor-
age capacity depends on the target temperature, the cold water to the data
center. It does only makes sense to implement large seasonal storage for low
target temperatures. By increasing the target temperature signiﬁcant energy
savings are possible.
In situations where one should choose among a number of solutions for the
same problem it always goes back to one thing - the economy! From the eco-
nomic standpoint, the large storage solutions are unfeasible due to component
prices and insuﬃcient power savings. The present value optima shows that the
layout has surprisingly small eﬀects on both power consumption and present
value. The system layout does not matter as long as it is not a 100% VCC
solution. The present value optima have small to medium free cooling capacity
and the smallest storage capacity. High target temperatures yield best present
values, with the least negative values.
A number of assumptions and simpliﬁcations has been made, which make the
simulations express trends more than ﬁnal results. The trends are applicable
for Danish climate or similar.

• Tsource = TE = −15 ◦C
• Tsink = TC = 30 ◦C
• Granryd et al. [17, p. 3:19]: ηCd = 0.830
• EES model: ηCd = 0.8307

Appendix B
Güntner dry cooler with double
coil
In order to choose some realistic UA-values for the calculations a real model
has been found. A dry cooler from Güntner, Figure B.1. It is a ﬁnned tube
heat exchanger with distribution manifolds in one end. Two fans are placed
above each section consisting of two heat exchangers placed in a V-shape.
Figure B.1: Güntner GFD Dry cooler (V-shape coil). Here as a 2x9 model.
The manufacturer does not provide the UA directly in the data sheet [25], but
refer to their calculation software. However in the data sheet nominal heat
transfer is stated for some test conditions. Ethylene-Glycol (34%) at 40/35 ◦C
heat exchange with inlet air at 25 ◦C. The volume ﬂow rates at which this
happens is also stated. The only unknowns are the air outlet temperature and
the UA-value, but it can be calculated with simple energy balances, equation
(B.1)-(B.3).
125

choose a larger type, more sections will be added to the heat exchanger - in-
creasing the heat transfer area. The fans and geometry are the same for each

These heat exchanges occupy a signiﬁcant ground area, an area that contains
both the heat exchangers but also the spacing between them that is needed
for them to operate properly. If the rows are placed too close disturbance can
occur due to the radiation from one and another. And also they can block
for needed airﬂow. Maybe one can not assume that the air inlet temperature
is ambient if the rows are too close. In Table B.2 the needed ground area is
estimated for the diﬀerent sizes, it is assumed that the spacing between the
rows is equal to the width of a V-shape section. It is found that the average
land use is 1.34 m2 for each installed kW/K.

1.34
The prices of such heat exchanges depends on the design (pass-through, pipe
dimensions, ﬁn distance etc.) which depends on the temperate level and needed
heat transfer capacity. To ﬁnd estimations of prices the GPC software from
Güntner has been used [39]. This software matches the available conﬁgurations
to a desired cooling water temperature, ambient temperature and nominal heat
rate. In Table B.3 models have been found ambient temperatures 5 K below
water outlet temperature and 1 MW heat rate. Catalogue prices are provided
by the software and is here used to estimate a price per 1 MW installed capacity.
UA values are calculated in the same way as in Table B.1.

For ∆Tw = 5 the needed UA for 1 MW heat rate is in range 200.2-214.8 kW/K,
where the range for ∆Tw = 15 is approximately 80 kW/K lower: 115.3-129.8
kW/K. The lower UA-values are reasonable as the temperature diﬀerence is
higher and therefore a smaller heat transfer area is needed, equation (B.3),
regardless of the temperature level. The higher the water return temperature
==PAGE:142==
128
APPENDIX B. GÜNTNER DRY COOLER WITH DOUBLE COIL
is the higher the UA has to be.
A similar trend is for the prices. Low ∆Tw needs a larger capital investment as
the needed heat transfer area is larger than the high ∆Tw. However it turns out
that the prices per installed overall heat transfer coeﬃcient, UA, are almost the
same for all six models found, Table B.4. The the cost is in the range 2605-3132
DKK per 1 kW/K. The averaged investment cost is 2828.5 DKK/(kW/K).
